HEADLINE: Fresh Look for the Fall Theater Season

BYLINE: By MICHAEL SOMMERS

 BODY:
The most celebrated play ever to originate in New Jersey is arguably Thornton Wilder's ''Our Town,'' which had a one-night-only shakedown showing at the McCarter Theater in Princeton in 1938, before moving to Boston for tryouts and ultimately to Broadway.
The hits keep on coming from the Garden State. Last fall, McCarter -- now McCarter Theater Center -- presented ''Vanya and Sonia and Masha and Spike,'' by Christopher Durang, which later won a Tony Award for best play on Broadway. And ''Newsies the Musical'' still bounds along Broadway after its premiere at Paper Mill Playhouse in 2011.
More than a dozen fresh plays and musicals, either brand-new works or those being seen for the first time in New Jersey, are expected to be among the productions at the state's theaters this fall. 
''There is nothing like producing new works -- it is like giving birth,'' said Cheryl Katz, the artistic director of Luna Stage in West Orange. Like many companies, Luna mixes new and vintage dramas in its season. Its newborn event this fall is written by Ben Clawson, whose play ''The Dangers of Electric Lighting'' was given its premiere by the company in 2011. Mr. Clawson's latest, ''The High Water Mark,'' is about two middle-aged women, longtime friends, who compare how their lives have turned out (Oct. 10 to Nov. 10).
In association with commercial producers, Paper Mill Playhouse is readying another potential Broadway-bound contender in Millburn: the musical ''Honeymoon in Vegas,'' which Andrew Bergman has shaped from his 1992 movie about a fellow who loses his fiancÃ©e to a gambler in a poker game (Sept. 26 to Oct. 27). Jason Robert Brown (''Parade'') provides the score, the ''Chaplin'' star Rob McClure portrays the unlucky groom and Tony Danza plays the card shark. Paper Mill then plans to revive Lionel Bart's Dickens musical ''Oliver!'' (Nov. 21 to Dec. 29).
At George Street Playhouse in New Brunswick, ''Gettin' the Band Back Together'' is a collaboration by Ken Davenport and a collective known as the Grundleshotz with the songwriter Mark Allen. Their musical concerns a middle-aged, recently unemployed New Jersey man who re-connects with his former garage-band buddies (Sept. 24 to Oct. 27). Alison Fraser will be in the cast, directed by John Rando. George Street then has the premiere of ''Clever Little Lies,'' Joe DiPietro's contemporary comedy-drama concerning marital fidelity (Nov. 19 to Dec. 22). Also in New Brunswick, ''Kansas City Swing,'' written by Ricardo Khan and Trey Ellis, with music by Bobby Watson, is a melding of 1940s baseball history, racial integration themes and jazz staged by Mr. Khan at Crossroads Theater Company (Oct. 10 to 27).
The Shakespeare Theater of New Jersey in Madison will introduce audiences to ''A Most Dangerous Woman,'' Cathy Tempelsman's bio-drama about the Victorian novelist George Eliot, directed by Richard Maltby Jr. (Sept. 18 to Oct. 12). Then the troupe presents ''Our Town'' (Oct. 17 to Nov. 17), followed by Shakespeare's far-flung saga of ''Pericles'' (Dec. 4 to 29).
A company that always delivers something new to its audiences, the New Jersey Repertory Company in Long Branch will stage John Biguenet's ''Broomstick,'' a poetic one-woman play regarding a witch (Sept. 19 to Oct. 13), as the first stop on the National New Play Network's Rolling World Premiere, and the state premiere of ''Middlemen,'' David Jenkins's comedy of corporate existence (Nov. 7 to Dec. 8).
Having begun its season with the current revival of David Auburn's Pulitzer-winning ''Proof'' directed by Emily Mann (through Oct. 6), McCarter Theater Center will present the state premiere of ''The White Snake,'' a mini-epic created from a Chinese myth by the writer-director Mary Zimmerman (Oct. 15 to Nov. 3).
The Two River Theater opens the fall with ''On Borrowed Time,'' Paul Osborn's cozy 1938 fantasy about a boy and his grandpa chasing the incarnation of Death up an apple tree in their backyard (Sept. 14 to Oct. 13). Joel Grey, who played the role of the boy in a Cleveland production in 1941, is directing the revival. Then the Obie Award-winning writer-actor Martin Moran will bring two solo pieces, ''The Tricky Part'' (Oct. 26 to Nov. 17) and ''All the Rage'' (Nov. 3 to 17), to Red Bank under the omnibus title ''A Map of the Soul.'' Acclaimed Off Broadway, these memoirs about personal forgiveness and redemption will be performed by Mr. Moran in repertory.
At the American Theater Group in Rahway, Jim Brochu will perform ''Character Man,'' his recent Off Broadway salute to song-and-dance featured players (Sept. 25 to Oct. 6). Then comes ''Bubble Boy,'' the world premiere of a musical comedy by Cinco Paul and Ken Daurio, the writers of the film ''Despicable Me,'' about the romantic troubles of a teenager born without an immune system (Nov. 6 to 24).
The Bickford Theater in Morristown will offer the local debut of Mr. DiPietro's ''The Last Romance,'' a 2008 comedy about an octogenarian's love affair (Sept. 26 to Oct. 13). Next it will stage a new whodunit by Paul Falzone, ''Sherlock Holmes: Knight's Gambit'' (Oct. 24 to Nov. 10), a coproduction with the Oldcastle Theater Company of Bennington, Vt.
At Premiere Stages at Kean University in Union, a new drama by Erik Gernand, ''The Beautiful Dark,'' studies a woman who fears that her teenager may be planning an act of terror (through Sept. 22). In Summit, Dreamcatcher Repertory Theater will offer the state's first look at Sharr White's ''The Other Place,'' which appeared on Broadway last season and depicts a brilliant woman coping with incipient dementia (Oct. 3 to 20). In Trenton, the Passage Theater Company will stage the premiere of E. M. Lewis's ''True Story,'' a thriller involving a ghost writer's collaboration with an acquitted murder defendant (Nov. 7 to 24).
Other companies are preparing works of various vintages. Centenary Stage Company in Hackettstown will produce Ira Levin's murderous thriller ''Deathtrap'' (Oct. 4 to 20). The Theater Project in Maplewood will present Steven Canny and John Nicholson's farcical three-actor version of ''The Hound of the Baskervilles'' (Sept. 26 to Oct. 13). East Lynne Theater Company in Cape May will resurrect ''The Late Christopher Bean,'' Sidney Howard's 1932 comedy-drama about a neglected painter and his masterpieces (Sept. 18 to Oct. 13). Cape May Stage, where Marc Camoletti's ''Boeing Boeing'' continues through Sept. 13, next schedules Mark St. Germain's recent Off Broadway success ''Freud's Last Session,'' about an encounter between the psychiatrist and the writer C. S. Lewis (Sept. 18 to Oct. 19). It will be followed by ''A Walk in the Woods,'' Lee Blessing's 1988 drama about the conversations between two arms negotiators (Oct. 23 to Nov. 16).
Appropriately, ''Our Town'' materializes at least twice this fall. In addition to the Shakespeare Theater of New Jersey's production, the great American drama will be revived by Surflight Theater in Beach Haven (Sept. 25 to Oct. 4). â--

HEADLINE: Bloomberg's Creature?

BYLINE: By GINIA BELLAFANTE

 BODY:
One morning last month, not long before Bill de Blasio was scheduled to appear in Greenwich Village to talk about the dangers of hospital closings -- something he has done during the mayoral campaign as regularly as others take their morning juice -- supporters of Christine C. Quinn staged a rival news conference to counter the criticism she has endured over the demise of St. Vincent's Hospital.
Former State Senator Thomas K. Duane was just beginning to speak on her behalf when a cluster of people who didn't care much for Ms. Quinn started to shout over him. An incensed man in his 80s emerged, began yelling and slapped two people -- one of them an intern for Ms. Quinn and another a state senator. 
The assailant, George Capsis, the publisher of a community newspaper, explained his derailment, telling reporters that his wife had just died in a hospital in the Bronx, far from where he lived. If St. Vincent's had remained open, he felt, he would have had more time with her during the final hours.
The photographs of an unhinged Mr. Capsis, a fist in the air, make an apt visual metaphor for the mood surrounding Ms. Quinn, who has fallen sharply in the polls in advance of the Democratic primary on Tuesday. They speak to an antipathy many New Yorkers have toward her that is at once visceral and vague and immune to mutation.
Ms. Quinn's unfavorables, to use the pollsters' argot, are high. In a New York Times/Siena College poll of 505 likely Democratic voters conducted in late August, 45 percent said their opinion of Ms. Quinn was unfavorable (putting her second only to Anthony D. Weiner by that measure). A striking 59 percent of labor union members held that position. More than 45 percent of those with college and graduate degrees shared a negative view of her. Men and women appear to dislike her in equal measure. More older voters dislike her than younger voters, and older voters, of course, vote in greater numbers.
Anger toward Ms. Quinn is readily in evidence. There are T-shirts that read ''Anybody but Quinn,'' part of a campaign to defeat her that is a joint effort of public-sector office workers and animal rights advocates upset over her unwillingness as City Council speaker to try to end the horse carriage trade. A Web site titled Quinn Hates Animals features pictures of her chosen to make her look so devoid of compassion that you imagine her denying a cool bowl of water to a panting retriever.
How has this come to pass, this intensity of distaste for a candidate whose greatest sin is not an authoritarian Giulianiesque vision for New York, but really not much of a vision at all?
While all the candidates in the Democratic field have variously compromised themselves for the purpose of political gain, the taint of the transactional has adhered with the most constancy to Ms. Quinn. She has been cast as the hack, the soul-seller, the expedient manipulator of self-interested deal-making. And yet for all that she is perceived as Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg's handmaiden and replica, she could not even engender enough reciprocal loyalty and affection to keep him from asking Hillary Rodham Clinton to consider entering the race.
When you ask people why they don't like Ms. Quinn, they cite her closeness to the mayor, her reversal on term limits and the incredibly long time it took her to support paid sick leave for privately employed workers. This in particular makes her seem not merely opportunistic but petty and unfeeling, especially given that her efforts to humanize herself to voters have turned on her struggles with addiction and her mother's early death from cancer. In a debate on Tuesday, when the new front-runner, Mr. de Blasio, asked Ms. Quinn why it took her three years to come around on sick leave, her maddening response was simply that the bill eventually passed.
For someone with a bully's reputation, Ms. Quinn can seem oddly prone to avoidance. At a rally for Mr. de Blasio in Cobble Hill in Brooklyn on Tuesday, Natalie Green Giles, a mother active in PTA circles and local politics, told me that Ms. Quinn had garnered a lot of ill will among public-school parents in her area when she failed to show up in the spring at an education forum at Public School 29 that was moderated by Diane Ravitch, the country's most vocal antagonist to the school reform movement championed by Mr. Bloomberg. (At the time, Ms. Quinn's campaign cited a scheduling conflict.)
Part of the problem of Ms. Quinn's appeal is that she has been in a continuing mode of response, first having had to contend with the threat of Mr. Weiner's surprise candidacy and then with the tireless and obviously resonant messaging of Mr. de Blasio. Even as she continues to steer the City Council, she is always reacting to an agenda rather than setting one; she is always having to defend herself -- and defensiveness in politics and in life rarely leaves people wanting more of you.
There are other aspects of Ms. Quinn's profile that fail to inspire potential supporters. I've had acquaintances tell me that they find her too provincial, too suburban, which is to say not quite sophisticated enough to manage the gleaming global city she is criticized for helping to create. Worldly intellectuals of the left have flocked to Mr. de Blasio: the celebrity economists Joseph E. Stiglitz and Jeffrey Sachs, and Katrina vanden Heuvel, editor and publisher of The Nation. Mr. de Blasio also has the support of George Soros.
Alternatively, among Ms. Quinn's most recent endorsements was one from George Takei of ''Star Trek.'' When the actor, who is well known in the gay rights movement, voiced his support for Ms. Quinn on his popular Facebook page, the value wasn't necessarily apparent. Fans expressed displeasure.
''Are you nuts?'' wrote one. ''The woman is Bloomberg's creature. Rather than listen to her speaking voice for four years, I'd move to Dubuque.''
E-mail: bigcity@nytimes.com

HEADLINE: China Looks Westward As It Bolsters Regional Ties

BYLINE: By JANE PERLEZ; Bree Feng contributed reporting.

 BODY:
ASTANA, Kazakhstan -- President Xi Jinping of China, evoking the camel caravans of the old Silk Road that traversed the ancient plains of Kazakhstan on their way from China to Europe, said Saturday that he wanted to create a contemporary version that would bind together China and its Central Asian neighbors.
Fresh from the Group of 20 summit meeting in St. Petersburg, Russia, Mr. Xi referred to Kazakhstan as an increasingly important energy supplier for China and an anchor for its new ''marching westwards'' policy, which looks to quickly strengthen economic and strategic relations with Central Asia. 
China remains dependent on the Middle East to feed its huge oil needs, but wants to diversify, experts say, so that more oil and gas providers are closer to home. Energy from Central Asia comes via land-based pipelines that are considered safer than the more vulnerable sea routes from the Middle East.
Mr. Xi is visiting four Central Asian countries on his current swing through the region: Turkmenistan, where he stopped last week; Uzbekistan; Kyrgyzstan, where he will attend the Shanghai Cooperation Organization meeting; and Kazakhstan. In each country, oil and gas, and regional security, are at the top of the agenda.
In a show of the importance of Kazakhstan's energy, President Nursultan A. Nazarbayev and Mr. Xi together pushed a button at the Palace of Independence here in Astana, the capital, to symbolically open a 700-mile pipeline that will take gas from the Caspian Sea in western Kazakhstan to the south. The pipeline, built jointly by the Chinese and the Kazakhs, will then connect to the vast Central Asia-China pipeline in Turkmenistan, and take gas all the way to China's coastal cities.
The two leaders signed trade and finance accords worth $30 billion, including loans from China's Development Bank and the Export-Import Bank.
''China highly values its friendship with these countries and takes them as a foreign policy priority,'' Mr. Xi said in a speech here at Nazarbayev University, founded by the president. He pledged support for each other's ''core interests'' and spoke of a ''strategic partnership with Central Asian countries.''
The old Silk Road, which took Chinese silk, porcelain and jade to Europe, started in his home province, Shaanxi, in central China, Mr. Xi said. ''I can almost hear the ring of the camel bells and the wisps of smoke in the desert,'' he said.
Under Mr. Xi, China has flexed its maritime strength in the waters surrounding it, challenging some American allies, including Japan and the Philippines, over territorial issues, and responding to President Obama's policy of refocusing American military and economic interests on the Asia-Pacific region.
But at the same time, Mr. Xi has sought to consolidate relations with Central Asia, a path that would broaden China's interests beyond its traditional base in the Asia-Pacific area and serve its vast energy needs as well.
The strategy also has the advantage of countering, and complementing, American plans for a ''new Silk Road'' announced by Secretary of State Hillary Rodham Clinton in 2011, said Wang Jisi, dean of the Center for International and Strategic Studies at Peking University. Mr. Wang is an architect of the ''march westwards'' policy, and an occasional adviser to the government on foreign affairs.
Mrs. Clinton proposed the ''new Silk Road'' for postwar Afghanistan, and said the United States would foster private sector investment in transportation and energy infrastructure throughout the region. But there is no need to wait for the Americans, Mr. Wang suggested.
Moreover, China does not need to limit itself to ''first becoming an Asia-Pacific power, then becoming a global power,'' Mr. Wang said in a paper last November for the International and Strategic Studies Report, a publication of Peking University, that was widely noted by American and Chinese foreign policy experts.
In Kazakhstan, China has made some important energy investments, including the purchase this year of an 8.4 percent stake in the Kashagan oil field in the Caspian Sea, a vast project being developed by an international consortium including KazMunaiGaz, Kazakhstan's state-owned energy company; Exxon Mobil; Royal Dutch Shell; and the Italian company Agip.
The final details of that purchase are almost complete, officials said. The oil field, which is scheduled to start operation next month, is described as the largest reserve of oil outside Saudi Arabia.
About 22 percent of Kazakhstan's oil production comes from joint ventures between Chinese and Kazakh companies, according to the Oil and Gas Ministry here. For Kazakhstan, China's new interests reverse an earlier policy of close relations with the West. ''It has found that China is an easier economic partner and has more cash,'' said Deirdre Tynan, Central Asia project director of the International Crisis Group. ''China is able to step in and provide massive loans without strings attached.''
In 2009, China stepped in to salvage Kazakhstan's banks, lending $5 billion to the state-owned Development Bank of Kazakhstan and $5 billion to KazMunaiGaz.

HEADLINE: Prices Are Rising for New Homes, and the Land They Are Built On

BYLINE: By SHAILA DEWAN

 BODY:
MINNEAPOLIS -- A few months ago, Michael N. Felix's phone started ringing again after four years of silence. Mr. Felix is a land broker whose business dried up when the housing market crashed. But with home prices now rising faster than anyone expected, builders are again looking for what, in the land trade, is referred to as dirt.
Already, developers report that the cost of land in the most desirable areas is double what it was two years ago. At least three golf courses in the Minneapolis-St. Paul area are being carved into millions of dollars' worth of residential lots. The race has even sent builders back to outer suburbs like Otsego, 30 miles from downtown Minneapolis, where bulldozers are laying the groundwork for four-bedroom houses with three-car garages, in subdivisions bordered by cornfields. 
''Lot buyers and sellers!!!!!!!!'' Mr. Felix's Web site reads. ''It is time to get moving again....!''
Or past time. The latest land rush is in full swing, as developers realize that they have failed to feed the zoning, permitting and mapping pipeline, which can take months or years to turn raw fields into buildable lots. They are realizing another thing, too: they have been sorely missed.
''For the first time, I've seen cities want to work to help figure it out, rather than doing us a favor all the time to let us develop,'' said Scott Carlston of Hunter Emerson, a development partnership. Hunter Emerson won a victory when the city of Eagan, a suburb of Minneapolis, allowed Parkview Golf Club to be converted into a high-end single-family subdivision.
The hunt for dirt is not limited to the Twin Cities. After builders across the country spent decades feeding acre after acre of raw land into the maw of demand for single-family homes, the housing crash left them with a land surplus so large that lots were selling for pennies on the dollar. At the peak of supply, in 2009, there were enough lots to last almost eight years, according to MetroStudy, a firm that tracks housing data. Now there is less than four year's worth, and only about a quarter of that is in the more desirable A- or B-rated locations.
''We have gone from a situation where five years ago everyone was saying, 'There's too many lots,' to today, builders are literally crying on our shoulder saying, 'There's not enough lots. We can't find any,''' said Bradley F. Hunter, the chief economist at MetroStudy.
The shortage of lots is slowing the housing recovery, the National Association of Home Builders said last week. In August, 59 percent of builders surveyed said lot supply was low or very low, the association said. Housing is a critical driver for the economy, not just because of the jobs and supplies needed to build homes but also the appliances and furnishings that new occupants buy.
At the peak of the housing boom, builders were finishing more than 1.6 million single-family houses a year. That number plunged to less than half a million during the recession. This year, the industry is on track to complete more than 570,000 homes, still substantially below the level considered necessary to replace aging homes and provide for new households. A return to more normal rates of construction would substantially lift the economy's anemic growth rate of about 2 percent over the last year.
Mr. Carlston said some cities in the Twin Cities area had adjusted their rules to allow fewer parking spaces or smaller lots. Otsego has lowered some of its development fees and allowed a developer to change an approved plan so that a partly built town house project could be finished with more salable detached homes. Rick Packer, a land development manager for Centra Homes, said some suburbs were relaxing requirements that homes be made of brick or stucco.
Even the Sierra Club, which once placed Minneapolis among the top 10 sprawl-threatened cities, has backed off a bit. An annual bike ride by the local chapter, once known as the ''Tour de Sprawl,'' has been given a less pejorative name and refocused to include not just threatened green space but what the group considers model development and transportation projects.
Mayor Mike Maguire of Eagan, a co-chairman of the Regional Council of Mayors Housing Initiative, said one reason his city had approved a land use change for the golf course was that so little new housing was built in the last few years. ''When there's no new development, you have stock that's increasingly out of date and that tends to bring your home values down,'' he said. ''That was one of the things we were hearing back from Realtors, was they had people who wanted to move to Eagan but couldn't find the home they wanted.''
Last year, Hunter Emerson agreed to pay $8.6 million for the golf course, wagering that the city would approve the land use change. The partnership sold the property to a national home builder for $13.1 million, Mr. Carlston said. The houses will cost from $400,000 to $700,000, he said.
The excess left from the boom -- land in various stages of development ranging from untouched to what builders call PVC farms, named for the hard plastic plumbing pipes that, with electrical lines, were virtually all that was on the lots -- is quickly being absorbed. Developers have gone from buying foreclosed acreage from banks to buying from farmers, family trusts, manufacturers and even homeowners with outdated homes on single lots.
''What we've seen is the inner ring of the suburbs, all those areas have come back,'' said Rod Just of Key Land Homes, a Twin Cities builder. ''The outer ring, they've taken just a little bit longer because of gas prices, but they're going to come back.''
For builders, there is even a sense of déjà vu. ''The new lots that are coming out,'' Mr. Just said, ''are almost the prices that they were in 2005 when everything crashed.''

GRAPHIC: PHOTO: A partly finished housing development in Otsego, Minn. Like other cities in the Twin Cities area, Otsego has relaxed development fees to encourage development. (PHOTOGRAPHS BY TIM GRUBER FOR THE NEW YORK TIMES) CHARTS: Fewer Places to Build: While housing construction and prices have been rising, the supply of vacant developed lots available for home-building is at its lowest since 2007. That shortage might be slowing the housing recovery, according to the National Association of Home Builders. (Sources: U.S. Census Bureau
 MetroStudy)

HEADLINE: David S. Landes, Historian And Author, Is Dead at 89

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS MARTIN

 BODY:
David S. Landes, a distinguished Harvard scholar of economic history, saw tidal movements in the rise of seemingly small things. He suggested that the development of eyeglasses made precision tools possible. Maybe, he said, using chopsticks helped Asian workers gain the manual dexterity needed to make microprocessors.
In his 482-page ''Revolution in Time: Clocks and the Making of the Modern World'' (1983), Professor Landes, who died last month at 89, examined the growth of the industrial age through the history of timepieces, tracing their origin to medieval European monasteries; monks, he wrote, needed something to tell them when to gather for a regular round of group prayer. 
To Professor Landes, the development of timepieces -- more than steamships -- drove the industrial age by molding the very culture of capitalism. Factory owners, for example, awarded watches to punctual workers, while workers bought watches to make sure they were not being misused by the factory clock.
Professor Landes was preoccupied by the importance of culture in shaping economic and social progress or stagnation. His most influential work, ''The Wealth and Poverty of Nations: Why Some Are So Rich and Some So Poor'' (1998), answered the question posed in its title (a play on that of Adam Smith's classic work) by pointing to the importance of the Protestant work ethic and European attitudes toward science and technology.
Mitt Romney, the Republican presidential nominee in 2012, acknowledged Professor Landes as an influence. ''There are superior cultures and ours is one of them,'' Mr. Romney wrote in his 2010 book, ''No Apology: The Case for American Greatness.'' ''As David Landes observed, 'Culture makes all the difference.' ''
Professor Landes's views lay behind a controversial remark Mr. Romney made in July 2012 at a campaign fund-raiser in Jerusalem. In a speech in which he mentioned ''The Wealth and Poverty of Nations,'' Mr. Romney suggested that a superior culture explains why Israelis are more economically successful than Palestinians. Palestinians called the remark racist and criticized Mr. Romney for not acknowledging the trade restrictions that Israel has imposed on them.
But in a joint statement issued to The Boston Globe, Professor Landes and his son, Richard, a historian at Boston University, expressed support for Mr. Romney and approved of his remarks about Israeli culture.
The statement also sought to pay Palestinians a compliment by lauding their culture and praising their economic success in comparison with that of other Arab peoples. ''Much of that comes from their close association with the Zionists,'' the statement said of Palestinians.
Professor Landes was often lumped with the branch of academia and politics known as neoconservativism, partly for his praise of the European model of development over those of other cultures. But his positions were not always predictable.
He split from conservative economists by questioning their view that free trade is always good for development. And even though he thought colonialism was not to blame for the stagnation of former colonies in Africa, Latin America and elsewhere, he compared Columbus's atrocities against indigenous people in the New World to Hitler's Holocaust.
Richard Landes said that his father died on Aug. 17 in Haverford, Pa., where he lived, and that his health had failed since his wife, the former Sonia Tarnopol, died in April. Besides his son, survivors include two daughters, Jane Foster and Alison Fiekowsky; eight grandchildren; and nine great-grandchildren.
David Saul Landes was born on April 29, 1924, on the kitchen table of his parents' home in the Seagate neighborhood of Coney Island in Brooklyn. His father, Harry, was a real estate investor.
As a youth, David built an immense vocabulary by religiously reading the dictionary. He skipped four grades on his way to the City College of New York, from which he graduated in 1942. The next year he earned a master's degree in history from Harvard and was drafted into the Army.
As it happened, he had been taking mail-order courses in cryptanalysis, and so he was assigned to the Signal Corps. He worked on deciphering Japanese messages about the atomic bombs dropped on Japan. He later worked on a history of German preparations for the invasion of Normandy.
After being honorably discharged, he returned to Harvard to work on his doctorate. His dissertation became his first book, ''Bankers and Pashas: International Finance and Economic Imperialism in Egypt.'' He received his Ph.D. in history from Harvard in 1953.
He also pursued his studies at Columbia University and taught there from 1952 to 1958. While there, he was a member of the Society of Fellows, which supported interdisciplinary studies, and in 1957-58 he was a fellow at the Center for Advanced Study in the Behavioral Sciences at Stanford University. He then joined the faculty at the University of California, Berkeley, where he was a professor of history and economics until 1964.
That year he wrote a letter to The New York Times criticizing the protests known as the free speech movement, which he called ''the most serious assault on academic freedom in America since the McCarthy era.''
He joined Harvard in 1964 as a professor of history and went on to hold appointments in political science and economics as well. Since 1987 he had been a senior fellow of Harvard's Society of Fellows, which was created as an alternative to the Ph.D. degree for exceptionally qualified candidates. At his retirement, in 1996, he was Coolidge Professor of History and an emeritus professor of economics.
In more than a half-dozen books and scores of articles, Professor Landes's writing was often as light as his subjects were heavy. Reviewing his 2006 book, ''Dynasties: Fortunes and Misfortunes of the World's Great Family Businesses,'' for The Times of London, Christopher Silvester described the writing as pithy, thoughtful and sprightly. The book offers 13 sketches of tycoons, including Henry Ford, John D. Rockefeller and Armand Peugeot.
In one scene Nathan Rothschild, of the legendary financial family, is hard at work at his desk in London. A peer of the realm is brought in. Rothschild, intent on his ledgers, invites him to take a seat. Offended, the visitor blusters about his high standing. ''Take two seats,'' Rothschild says.

HEADLINE: Tuition Aid From a Zombie Elf

BYLINE: By BRENDAN SPIEGEL

 BODY:
Douglas Johnson began his sophomore year at the State University of New York at Oswego last week in an enviable position. He made the dean's list his freshman year and expects to graduate early with a double major in psychology and public justice. Even better, his family back in Watertown, N.Y., didn't have to take out any loans this fall, thanks to a scholarship that covers about half of his tuition.
And for that he has Glissa, the Traitor -- a zombie elf -- to thank.
''Friends and parents had told me to look for scholarships in everything,'' Mr. Johnson, 18, recalled. ''But I was surprised that there is a scholarship just for Magic players.'' 
Last year, Mr. Johnson received one of the first two grants given by Gamers Helping Gamers, a nonprofit organization founded by a group of successful young New Yorkers to assist a very specific group of students: those who play the fantasy trading card game Magic: The Gathering.
A cousin to role-playing games like Dungeons & Dragons, Magic pits two players in strategic combat, each armed with colorful cards representing creatures, spells and magical lands. In a twist that has turned the game into a global phenomenon and its publisher, Wizards of the Coast, into a juggernaut, players collect and trade cards to customize their decks. Magic is fundamentally a game of skill, but a stronger hand might be one $3.99 booster pack away.
Twenty years after Magic was introduced, it is more than a fringe pastime. Game shops and comic book stores like Kings Games in Brooklyn and Get There Games on Staten Island hold weekly Friday Night Magic events. And Wizards of the Coast hosts an international Pro Tour with purses that can reach $250,000.
The founders of Gamers Helping Gamers are stars in this world. By day, Jon Finkel is a partner in the hedge fund Landscape Capital. But he leads another life in the realm of Magic, as an international champion and Hall of Fame player who is frequently asked to sign fans' cards at tournaments and has even been immortalized in a card bearing his likeness.
The idea for the scholarship, Mr. Finkel said, was to find highly intelligent students who could benefit from a little extra engagement.
He coasted through high school in New Jersey, he said, never really exerting himself, then failed and had to leave Rutgers before returning to graduate.
''There was just nothing enjoyable or fun about high school, so I got my mental stimulation elsewhere,'' Mr. Finkel, 35, said. ''I would play Magic until midnight and never do my homework.''
Mr. Johnson could relate. ''My senior year, I'll admit I was pretty lazy,'' he recalled. ''I did nothing but float by. I got A's and B's, but I definitely didn't do the best I could.''
He won his scholarship -- $5,000 a year for college -- after writing essays about playing Magic in his small-town high school cafeteria and about his favorite card: Glissa, the Traitor. He said he knew about Mr. Finkel from watching him play in tournaments streamed online, never suspecting that the champion's winnings would soon pay for his own education.
''When a group of your biggest role models gives you a bunch of money, that motivates you to do really well,'' Mr. Johnson said.
The scholarship fund came about after Timothy McKenna, another of the founders, floated the idea to a group of friends who play together once or twice a week in New York City, usually at Mr. Finkel's roomy SoHo apartment.
While passing cards around on a rainy evening this June, Mr. McKenna, Mr. Finkel and their cohort explained that they regularly encountered teenagers at tournaments who were extremely smart, but not the type of extroverts with hordes of extracurricular activities that college admissions officers value.
''There are definitely a lot of scholarship opportunities for people who are more outgoing,'' Mr. McKenna, a 37-year-old economic consultant, said. ''We have an applicant pool that isn't being reached by a lot of scholarships, but should be.''
Throughout the evening, more friends popped in, looking for a match. But first, two of them joined Mr. Finkel and Mr. McKenna -- with a fifth piped in via iPhone -- as they gathered to review this year's applications. Amid friendly trash talk about the results of recent competitions, they touched on financial need, SAT scores and grade point averages, but saved most of their enthusiasm for the applicants' essays peppered with terms like ''deathtouch,'' ''planeswalker'' and ''hexproof.''
''Interestingly enough, his favorite Magic cards are the Shock Lands, which I love,'' Mr. Finkel said, as he re-reviewed one applicant's submission. ''But then I also loved one of the other candidates' essays, where he said that his least favorite cards are the Shock Lands. They both made good points.''
Impressed by an essay about lessons learned from nearly a decade playing Magic, the group awarded this year's sole new scholarship to Isaac Goldstein, a rising sophomore studying math at Lewis & Clark College in Portland, Ore.
The awards are not limited to those whose promise has been overlooked. Dylan Fay, last year's other award winner, was already excelling at the University of Florida and applying to law school. ''I had to buy study books, practice tests, visit all the law schools, and the applications cost about $100 each,'' said Mr. Fay, who used his $5,000 award to cover those costs. ''Without this scholarship I don't think all that would have been possible.''
Mr. Fay started Yale Law School last week after graduating with a triple major in English, classics and history. ''I'm probably never going to have time ever again to devote to it like I did in high school,'' he said of Magic, ''but I think I'll always go back to it from time to time.''
That's perfectly fine with Gamers Helping Gamers. ''The scholarship is not set up to find a guy or girl who devotes their whole life to Magic,'' said Mr. McKenna, who plans to play in a Pro Tour qualifier next week at the Hotel Pennsylvania in Manhattan; the winner will advance to a professional event in Valencia, Spain. Rather, the friends see their fund as an opportunity to help young people within this tight-knit community build a successful life outside of Magic. In the future, they hope to offer career advice and help with skills like résumé building.
''When we started playing Magic it was brand-new,'' Mr. Finkel said. ''There weren't players in their mid-30s. Now we're in a place to help out, and in 10, 20 years, you'll probably see a lot of Magic players in senior positions. They'll be in a position to help these young kids, to understand the ways in which they'll work well and won't work well, and maybe get a lot more out of them.''
With their scholarship winner selected, Mr. Finkel and friends began eagerly tearing open packs of new Magic cards and passing them around the table in rapid succession, preparing to enter the battlefield.

HEADLINE: House Republicans in California Find a Struggle on Immigration

BYLINE: By JENNIFER MEDINA

 BODY:
HANFORD, Calif. -- When Representative David Valadao came out in favor of a path to citizenship for millions of people living in the country illegally at an open forum this summer, the crowd erupted in applause, and young activists nearly knocked one another down as they rushed to embrace him.
Since then, Mr. Valadao, a freshman Republican, has become a bit of an unlikely celebrity among Latino farmworkers and their families in California's Central Valley, where immigration has shaped the agricultural industry for decades.
As the immigration debate simmers in Washington, California Republicans face a very different reality than a majority of their party colleagues, who elsewhere largely represent districts that are overwhelmingly white. 
More than half of California's 15 Republican members of Congress have districts that are at least 30 percent Latino, making them potentially vulnerable to Democratic challenges and prime targets for those pushing for a path to citizenship. In Mr. Valadao's district, Latinos make up 70 percent of the population.
With Republicans all but relegated to a minority party in California -- Democrats hold every statewide elected office, maintain a large majority in the State Legislature and hold a vast majority of Congressional seats -- many here blame the party's decline on its stance on immigration.
''We're finally breaking out of denial,'' said Allan Hoffenblum, a longtime Republican consultant in state politics. ''Until we solve our Latino problem, we're not going to be a viable state party, and it's hard to understand how that's possible without fixing immigration.''
Mr. Valadao, the son of Portuguese immigrants, is one of a few Republicans in the state offering full-throated support for sweeping changes to the country's immigration laws. Representatives Jeff Denham and Devin Nunes, whose districts include northern stretches of the state's agricultural center, have said they would support some kind of legalization process for the nation's roughly 11 million illegal immigrants, the most divisive issue in the debate.
All of this has put pressure on Representative Kevin McCarthy, a Republican whose district is about one-third Latino and includes part of Bakersfield, a working-class, immigrant-heavy city that Mr. Valadao also represents. As the majority whip, the third-ranking leader of the Republican-controlled House, Mr. McCarthy is expected to play an important role in deciding which legislative proposals end up being voted on and which are squelched this fall.
In the past, Mr. McCarthy has called the immigration process broken, saying that the priority must be on securing borders and that he is opposed to an amnesty. Aides to the lawmaker did not respond to repeated requests for an interview.
One commercial appearing on cable television in his district attacks ''obstructionists'' blocking an immigration overhaul. Another accuses him of ''talking about legalizing 11 million illegal aliens, making it easier for them to take jobs.''
After a union-backed protest in Mr. McCarthy's district in August, demonstrators marched into his office with pictures of immigrants, along with farm tools and produce, in an attempt to illustrate their impact on the community here. In the arid Central Valley, where dairy farms and peach and almond orchards stretch for miles, immigrants have provided the steadiest source of agricultural and service employees.
Mr. McCarthy told a local radio station last week that most of the protesters had come from outside his district. Mr. McCarthy also faces pressure from those against illegal immigration. ''He's shown that he is flexible on immigration and has said we need to be more open to foreign workers, but the great irony is that he's in a district with high unemployment,'' said Joe Guzzardi, the national media director for Californians for Population Stabilization, which opposes any kind of citizenship for those in the country illegally.
Advocates of a comprehensive immigration overhaul argue that California is a cautionary tale for Republicans in the rest of the country and that if the party does not help pass legislation this year, it will risk losing Latino votes for decades to come. Frank Sharry, the executive director of America's Voice, which is pushing for an immigration overhaul, points to 1994 as the start of problems for the state Republican Party. Pete Wilson, the governor at the time, led the campaign for a state ballot initiative severely limiting access to public services, including schools and emergency medical care, for illegal immigrants. Voters approved the measure, but it was ruled unconstitutional by federal courts.
''California became one of the bluest states in the country because the Republican Party followed Pete Wilson off the anti-immigration cliff,'' Mr. Sharry said. ''McCarthy isn't going to want to lose more seats, and he has to see that members who are becoming champions of immigration reform aren't just living to tell about it, but they're getting love letters.''
Across the state, other Republicans have softened their stance on immigration. Representative Ken Calvert, whose district covers Riverside County, has said he supports citizenship for those who were brought to the country as children and would consider some kind of legal status for others. Representative Gary G. Miller, whose district in San Bernardino, east of Los Angeles, is roughly 50 percent Latino and considered a prime target by Democrats, removed several strongly worded statements against immigration from his Web site this year.
Mr. Valadao's experience during an open house one recent evening illustrates the competing pressures. Minutes before a group of young students thanked him effusively for his immigration stance, two longtime residents denounced him, saying he was ''rewarding lawbreakers'' and ''stealing American jobs.''
He politely argued that illegal immigrants already made up about half of the agricultural employees in the area and that he had heard from dozens of farmers who said they could not fill jobs fast enough. But neither woman appeared swayed.
It is a point he has grown practiced at making. ''It's complicated, but the more I looked at it here, the more convinced I became that we need to give some kind of hope to people who are working here, supporting their families and doing everything else right,'' Mr. Valadao said in an interview at his district office in Hanford, a small farming town in the middle of the state.
Angelica Salas, the executive director of the Coalition for Humane Immigrant Rights of Los Angeles, said Republicans were ''sort of wedged between a rock and a hard place of their own doing.'' The coalition organized dozens of events singling out Republican congressmen last month.
She said Mr. McCarthy had met with her and other activists and signaled that he was open to supporting citizenship. ''What I see him trying to deal with is the recognition that there's a real interdependence on immigrant labor in his district,'' Ms. Salas said, ''but also some very shrill Republicans who either don't get it or don't care.''
While Mr. Valadao said he did not have plans to increase pressure on his Republican colleagues, other local leaders have been more willing to openly voice criticism.
''We have people who graduated from our colleges who can't get jobs because they can't get citizenship,'' said Mayor Harvey L. Hall of Bakersfield, a Republican who spoke onstage at a protest directed at Mr. McCarthy. ''All you have to do is go out on the weekend, and you can see that our growth as a city depends on the immigrant population.''
For the most part, immigration campaigners are counting their successes. By one tally, about two dozen House Republicans have said they might support a path to citizenship. But Mr. Nunes, who is included in that number, said the attention focused on Mr. McCarthy could backfire, as Republicans in other parts of the country see unions and other Democratic allies focus on immigration as a partisan issue.
''Making a lot of noise and ruckus with someone open to change is just not credible -- it makes people harden their stance,'' Mr. Nunes said. ''I want to come up with a way to get something done, but it's looking like people just want the issue as a piñata for political rhetoric.''

HEADLINE: New Dance: From Jetés to Jookin

BYLINE: By SIOBHAN BURKE

 BODY:
September
BEAT FESTIVAL When you think of Downtown Brooklyn's cultural attractions, large, expensive buildings come to mind (the Barclays Center, the Brooklyn Academy of Music). Stephen Shelley looked elsewhere when organizing this festival of site-specific performance, which pops up at plazas and other communal spaces throughout Downtown Brooklyn and nearby neighborhoods. One palatial landmark does play a role in this second edition: the Brooklyn Museum, where an opening-night bash, among other events, features the dance artists Yanira Castro, Third Rail Projects, LeeSaar The Company and Storyboard P. Thursday-Sept. 21. Various sites, (347) 762-3281, beatbrooklyn.com.
TIME-BASED ART FESTIVAL If you're curious about contemporary performance in Belgium, Portugal, Morocco, Algeria, Croatia, France, Minneapolis, San Francisco or Portland, this festival, hosted by the Portland Institute of Contemporary Art, is a good place to be. This year's thoroughly international projects confront everything from ''fantasies around death'' (Laura Arrington and Jesse Hewit's ''Adult'') to perennial questions of what it means to look and be looked at (Ivana Müller's ''We Are Still Watching''). Thursday-Sept. 22. (503) 224-7422, pica.org. 
FALL FOR DANCE The festival kicks off its 10th anniversary with two free outdoor performances at Central Park's Delacorte Theater (Sept. 16-17), featuring New York City Ballet, Ronald K. Brown/Evidence, the Paul Taylor Dance Company and Streb Extreme Action. The samplers (and annual scrambling for cheap tickets) continue a week later at City Center (Sept. 25-Oct. 5), with 20 international companies on five programs. A few highlights: the imaginative Irish dancer Colin Dunne, the maverick tap artist Michelle Dorrance and commissioned works by ballet's most sought-after men of the moment, Justin Peck and Liam Scarlett. 131 West 55th Street, (212) 581-1212, nycitycenter.org.
NEW YORK CITY BALLET Whether you call it the Koch Theater or the State Theater (its original name, still preferred by some), City Ballet has been in its Lincoln Center home for nearly 50 years. In honor of the milestone, the company will perform some 50 ballets during its 2013-14 season. Three of those -- world premieres by Benjamin Millepied, Angelin Preljocaj and Justin Peck -- will be unveiled, outfitted in designer costumes, at the fall gala, Sept. 19. Works by Balanchine, Robbins, Alexei Ratmansky, Christopher Wheeldon and Mauro Bigonzetti complete the fall lineup (through Oct. 13). Then, like clockwork, comes Balanchine's ''Nutcracker'' (which predates the State Theater by 10 years), Nov. 29-Jan. 4. David H. Koch Theater, Lincoln Center, (212) 496-0600, nycballet.com.
JAPAN SOCIETY Basil Twist may technically be a puppeteer, but his work displays a knowledge of the human body, its physics and anatomy, that rivals any dancer's or choreographer's. Bookending the Japan Society's 60th-anniversary season are his acclaimed ''Dogugaeshi'' (Sept. 18-22) and, next spring, a more conventional kind of dance, Luca Veggetti's ''Pleiades,'' featuring the percussionist Kuniko Kato and the former Nederlands Dans Theater member Megumi Nakamura (May 2-3). 333 East 47th Street, (212) 715-1258, japansociety.org.
THE CHOCOLATE FACTORY THEATERThe perception that Long Island City is ''far away'' prevents some New Yorkers from visiting this excellent little theater. It's worth bridging the psychological distance for the distinctive, adventurous artists that the artistic director Brian Rogers has assembled, including Megan V. Sprenger (Sept. 18-21), Karinne Keithley Syers (Oct. 2-5), Will Rawls (Nov. 13-16), Jon Kinzel (Dec. 4-7) and Liz Santoro (May 14-17). Through June 21. 5-49 49th Avenue, Long Island City, Queens, (212) 352-3101, chocolatefactorytheater.org.
JOFFREY BALLET For historical accuracy, no ''Rite of Spring'' can compete with the Joffrey's. The meticulous reconstruction by Millicent Hodson and Kenneth Archer of the Nijinsky original (which, as you've probably heard, turns 100 this year) completes its national tour at the company's home theater, sharing a Russian Masters program with Balanchine's ''Allegro Brillante'' and Yuri Possokhov's ''Bells.'' Sept. 19-22. Auditorium Theater, Chicago, (800) 982-2787, joffrey.com.
CROSSING THE LINE Labels like ''dance'' and ''theater'' don't go very far in describing what happens at the French Institute Alliance Française's interdisciplinary festival. Its astute curators Lili Chopra, Simon Dove and Gideon Lester seek out work that says what it needs to say by whatever means necessary. This year's dancier events include a new piece by the Moroccan artist Bouchra Ouizguen, Nora Chipaumire's ''riot'' (her answer to ''The Rite of Spring'') and ''a (micro) history of world economics, danced'' by the French playwright and director Pascal Rambert. Sept. 19-Oct. 13. Various sites, (212) 355-6160, fiaf.org.
PEAK PERFORMANCES New Jersey holds its own as a dance-going destination largely thanks to Jedediah Wheeler, the artistic director of this performing arts series at Montclair State University. Liz Gerring gets the season started with her expansive, athletic movement in the world premiere of ''glacier'' (Sept. 19-22). The months ahead bring Douglas Dunn's new ''Aubade'' with video by Charles Atlas (Jan. 24-Feb. 1) and Wayne McGregor's London-based company, Random Dance, in ''Atomos,'' his latest exploit at the intersection of technology and the hyperkinetic human form (March 15-23). Alexander Kasser Theater, Montclair, N.J., (973) 655-5112, peakperfs.org.
NEW YORK LIVE ARTS While some things have changed at the former Dance Theater Workshop since its rebirth as New York Live Arts in 2011, we can still count on Carla Peterson, the artistic director, for her bold, diverse programming. The new season -- a balance of local and international, rookie and veteran artists -- opens with ''The Inkomati (dis)cord'' by Boyzie Cekwana (from South Africa) and Panaibra Canda (from Mozambique), part of Crossing the Line Sept. 25-26. New works from John Jasperse, Kimberly Bartosik, Donna Uchizono and many others are also in store. The Replay Series brings back productions worthy of a longer life, like Big Dance Theater's 2012 ''Ich, Kürbisgeist'' (Oct. 31-Nov. 9) and Ms. Uchizono's 1999 ''State of Heads'' (Dec. 7). Through May 31. 219 West 19th Street, (212) 924-0077, newyorklivearts.org.
PACIFIC NORTHWEST BALLET Peter Boal's multifaceted season begins with a Twyla Tharp triptych: the return of ''Nine Sinatra Songs,'' the company premiere of ''Brief Fling'' and a world premiere to music by the venerable New Orleans composer Allen Toussaint (Sept. 27-Oct. 6). Jumping ahead to the spring season, Mr. Boal offers a more unorthodox combination with his Director's Choice program (March 14-23): Susan Marshall's ''Kiss,'' Molissa Fenley's ''State of Darkness'' and a new work by the Chicago choreographer Alejandro Cerrudo. Through June 8. Seattle, (206) 441-2424, pnb.org.
October
ABRONS ARTS CENTER In addition to American Realness in January, a few intriguing productions will be passing through the Henry Street Settlement's arts wing this fall, including a new work by Pavel Zustiak (Oct. 2-12), Miguel Gutierrez's ''myendlesslove'' (publicized as ''not quite a solo performance about sex, desire and objectification,'' Oct. 9-19) and Arturo Vidich's ''142241,'' which hinges on our willingness to believe in invisible worlds (Nov. 14-16). Abrons Arts Center, (866) 811-4111, abronsartscenter.org.
BILL T. JONES/ARNIE ZANE DANCE COMPANY Mr. Jones's meditation on ''The Rite of Spring,'' a collaboration with Anne Bogart and SITI Company, has its New York premiere Oct. 3-5 at the Brooklyn Academy of Music, followed by its West Coast premiere at San Francisco's Yerba Buena Center for the Arts, Oct. 11-13. Also at Yerba Buena, he offers ''/Time: Study 1'' (Oct. 8-9), building on last year's ''Story/Time,'' which employed chance operations in the spirit of John Cage. The company's 30th-anniversary tour also heads to the Dance Center of Columbia College Chicago (Oct. 24-26) and Boston's Institute of Contemporary Art (Feb. 21-23). newyorklivearts.org.
JOANNA HAIGOOD After last year's ''Paseo'' -- a site-specific salute to the South Bronx and its Latin music renaissance of the 1940s, '50s and '60s -- Ms. Haigood returns to the neighborhood and moves on to the 1970s. In ''The Bronx Revolution and the Birth of Hip Hop,'' presented (free of charge) by Dancing in the Streets, she joins forces with Theodore Livingston (the D.J. better known as GrandWizzard Theodore) and the photographer Joe Conzo Jr. for an afternoon of multimedia storytelling by veteran hip-hop dancers, graffiti artists and emcees. Oct. 5. Starting at Casita Maria Center for Arts and Education, 928 Simpson Street, Bronx, dancinginthestreets.org.
STEVE PAXTON The Dia Art Foundation embarks on its two-part tribute to Mr. Paxton, the latest in its thoughtful series of postmodern performance retrospectives. The septuagenarian improviser (best known as the pioneer of Contact Improvisation) and his longtime collaborator Lisa Nelson bring their 2002 ''Night Stand'' to Dia:Chelsea for its United States premiere (Oct. 10-19). We'll have to wait awhile for part two, the full survey of Mr. Paxton's work, which comes to Dia:Beacon next fall. 535 West 22nd Street, (212) 989-5566, diaart.org.
ON THE BOARDS This Seattle institution, directed by Lane Czaplinski, supports both local and international artists prone to taking big risks. First in its dance lineup is Heather Kravas, who after years in New York has moved back to her native coast. At once wild and militant, she commands cohesion from four dancers in ''the quartet'' (Oct. 10-13). The season continues with Gregory Maqoma, Zoe/Juniper and Alain Buffard, to name just a few, through May 11. Behnke Center for Contemporary Performance, Seattle, (206) 217-9888, ontheboards.org.
NEXT WAVE FESTIVAL Between the Brooklyn Academy of Music's three theaters, there's a lot to keep up with at its annual fall festival. For example: at the Howard Gilman Opera House, the United States premieres of William Forsythe's ''Sider'' (Oct. 9-12) and Hofesh Shechter's ''Sun'' (Nov. 14-16); at the more intimate BAM Fisher, new works by Brian Brooks (Oct. 22-26), John Heginbotham (Oct. 29-Nov. 2), Kate Weare (Nov. 6-9) and Susan Marshall (Nov. 20-23); and at the BAM Harvey Theater, David Dorfman Dance (Oct. 16-19) and the contemporary circus duo Zimmermann & de Perrot (Oct. 23-26). Through Dec. 14. Various sites, (718) 636-4100, bam.org.
SAN FRANCISCO BALLET The company arrives at Lincoln Center for two weeks (Oct. 16-27) bearing an embarrassment of ballet riches: eight New York premieres, including works by Alexei Ratmansky, Wayne McGregor, Helgi Tomasson (the artistic director) and Christopher Wheeldon, whose opulent ''Cinderella'' gets a week to itself. Later in the season, back at home, Mr. Ratmansky's ''Shostakovich Trilogy,'' created for American Ballet Theater, will have its West Coast premiere, April 2. (415) 865-2000, sfballet.org.
ROSAS The words ''late medieval music'' might not send you running to the box office, but in this case -- as with anything involving this Belgian company and its visionary choreographer, Anne Teresa De Keersmaeker -- they should. In her recent companion pieces ''En Atendant'' and ''Cesena'' (presented on separate programs at the Next Wave Festival, Oct. 19-20), Ms. De Keersmaeker explores the haunting 14th-century musical style known as ars subtilior, taking us on dual journeys from light into darkness and darkness into light. In ''Cesena,'' the Antwerp-based choral group Graindelavoix joins the dancers onstage. bam.org.
November
MATTHEW BOURNE Following up on his cheeky ''Nutcracker!'' (1992) and his gender-bending ''Swan Lake'' (1995), the British choreographer offers the latest in his trilogy of extravagantly reimagined Tchaikovsky ballets. His ''Sleeping Beauty,'' which opened to mixed reviews in London last year, hurtles from the Victorian gothic bedroom of Aurora's youth to the bondage-themed nightclub where she gets married 100 years later. After its New York premiere at City Center (Oct. 23-Nov. 3), the production heads to the Kennedy Center (Nov. 12-17) and the Music Center in Los Angeles (Nov. 21-Dec. 1); new-adventures.net.
KEIGWIN + COMPANY Dependably light and fun, clever and carefree -- but never at the expense of clean, eye-catching structure -- Larry Keigwin has been thriving in the dance-making business for a decade. For his company's 10th anniversary (Oct. 29-Nov. 3), he presents the oldest work in his repertory, ''Mattress Suite,'' and his 2004 ''Natural Selection,'' alongside the New York premiere of ''Girls,'' complete with sparkling rain curtains and Frank Sinatra tunes. The Joyce Theater, 175 Eighth Avenue, at 19th Street, Chelsea; (212) 242-0800, joyce.org.
AMERICAN BALLET THEATER The company's fall season, usually far too short, happily expands from one week to two. It also relocates to a bigger stage, from City Center to New York City Ballet's turf, the David H. Koch Theater. The world premiere of ''The Tempest,'' by the resident choreographer Alexei Ratmansky, will surely benefit from the extra space, as will the revivals of Michel Fokine's ''Sylphides,'' Twyla Tharp's ''Bach Partita'' and Mark Morris's ''Gong.'' Oct. 30-Nov. 10. abt.org.
PERFORMA RoseLee Goldberg's ''visual art performance'' biennial is back, having downsized (a good idea) just slightly, with more than 100 events in more than 40 locations over three and a half weeks (Nov. 1-24). The dance presentations (of those that have been announced) include Maria Hassabi's ''Premiere'' at the Kitchen (Nov. 6-9), Noé Soulier's ''Ideography'' at Danspace Project (Nov. 7) and Boris Charmatz's ''Musée de la Danse'' at the Museum of Modern Art, which re-envisions the museum as a malleable, animated space (Oct. 18-Nov. 3). (212) 366-5700, performa-arts.org.
DANSPACE PROJECT Judy Hussie-Taylor's innovative Platform series, in which choreographers serve as curators for several weeks at a time, appears to be on hiatus. The fall season, more loosely structured, features premieres by Rashaun Mitchell and Silas Riener (Nov. 14-16) and Cori Olinghouse (Dec. 12-14), plus a reprise of Katy Pyle's ''Firebird: A Ballez.'' Okwui Okpokwasili and Vanessa Anspaugh are among the thought-provoking artists scheduled for 2014. The Platform format resumes in May with ''Diary of an Image,'' four weeks devoted to the work of DD Dorvillier (May 19-June 14). St. Mark's Church, 131 East 10th Street, East Village; (866) 811-4111, danspaceproject.org.
WALKER ART CENTER Rapturously received in Europe, Jérôme Bel's ''Disabled Theater'' -- his collaboration with Theater Hora, a Swiss company of disabled actors -- arrives at this dynamic Twin Cities theater (Nov. 21-23) by way of New York Live Arts (Nov. 12-17). In more local news, the popular Choreographers' Evening returns on Nov. 30 to showcase rising talent from Minneapolis, selected this year by Chris Yon and Taryn Griggs. The daredevil duo Hijack, an influential force on the city's dance scene, celebrates 20 years, Dec. 5-7. (612) 375-7600, walkerart.org.
MARK MORRIS Spiritual transcendence, the theme of Lincoln Center's White Light Festival, is a tall order, but many would claim that Mr. Morris can deliver with his ''L'Allegro, il Penseroso ed il Moderato.'' His company makes its White Light debut with that work, widely considered his greatest, Nov. 21-23. Another Handel score inspires Mr. Morris's next project, ''Acis and Galatea,'' which has its world premiere at Cal Performances April 25-27. markmorrisdancegroup.org.
December
ALVIN AILEY AMERICAN DANCE THEATER At a 55-year-old institution, where leaders tend to stick around for several decades, two years isn't very long. In that time, the artistic director Robert Battle has considerably refurbished the company's repertory, bringing in big names from abroad (Jiri Kylian, Ohad Naharin) and invigorating young voices like Kyle Abraham. He continues in that vein this season with a world premiere by Aszure Barton and two company premieres: Bill T. Jones's classic ''D-Man in the Waters'' and ''Chroma'' by the brainy British choreographer Wayne McGregor. Dec. 4-Jan. 5. (212) 581-1212, alvinailey.org.
TERE O'CONNOR After 30 years of making dances, how does a choreographer avoid repeating himself? One approach is to work with different groups of dancers, which Mr. O'Connor has done in three works -- ''Secret Mary,'' ''poem'' and ''Untitled'' -- over the past two years. Taking the process further, he now collapses those three into a fourth; like particles colliding, their disparate elements should fuse into something delightfully unexpected. The resulting composite, ''Bleed,'' has its world premiere at the Next Wave Festival, Dec. 11-14. bam.org.
JUILLIARD DANCE The trailblazing German choreographer Pina Bausch owed her dance education, in part, to Juilliard. Partnering with her other alma mater, the Folkwangschule in Essen, Germany, the conservatory reconstructs her 1975 ''Wind von West'' (''Wind From the West'') as part of its New Dances program, becoming the first United States institution -- and only the third in the world, after the Paris Opéra Ballet and her Tanztheater Wuppertal -- to perform Ms. Bausch's work. Dec. 11-15. Peter Jay Sharp Theater, Lincoln Center, (212) 721-6500, juilliard.edu/newdances.
January
FOCUS DANCE The buying and selling of live performance is in full force this month, thanks to the annual conference of the Association of Performing Arts Presenters. Amid what often feels like a cultural meat market, this showcase (with a sweeping ''focus'' on American choreographers) is a felicitous recent development, bringing together compelling artists who you don't usually see back to back. This year's curator, Laurie Uprichard, assembles some particularly interesting pairings, like Vicky Shick with Doug Elkins and Yvonne Rainer with Urban Bush Women. Jan. 7-12. joyce.org.
AMERICAN REALNESS Plans are still under way for Ben Pryor's fifth annual festival of contemporary performance at Abrons Arts Center, but if it's anything like past years, we can expect 11 days packed with smart, strong-minded artists who aren't afraid of making a mess. Confirmed, so far, are Michelle Boulé's ''Wonder,'' Juliana F. May's ''Commentary = not thing'' and Adrienne Truscott's ''Too Freedom.'' There will, no doubt, be much more, maybe too much; over-stimulation comes with the Realness territory. Jan. 9-19. tbspMGMT.com.
LIAM SCARLETT America has latched onto this British choreographer, a former dancer with the Royal Ballet who, last year, became its artist in residence at the age of 26. Having created two pieces for Miami City Ballet, he moves on to New York City Ballet, making his choreographic debut there on Jan. 31. His first work for San Francisco Ballet has its premiere on April 29.
February
PAM TANOWITZ There are two opportunities this month to see Ms. Tanowitz's exacting, elaborate choreography: during the Joyce Theater debut of her company (Feb. 4-6) and at Florence Gould Hall, where the small but highly regarded New York Theater Ballet performs her ''Short Memory,'' praised by the New York Times critic Brian Seibert for its ''brilliant use of stage space'' (Feb. 28-March 1).
ROYAL NEW ZEALAND BALLET Yes, it's been 13 years, but for some of us Ethan Stiefel will always be Cooper Nielsen in the 2000 teen dance movie ''Center Stage.'' Of course, he was also a principal dancer with American Ballet Theater, then he took off for New Zealand to become the artistic director of this company. Touring the United States for the first time under his leadership, the troupe arrives at the Joyce Theater (after stopping at the Music Center in Los Angeles, Jan. 31-Feb. 2). Feb. 12-16. joyce.org.
92ND STREET Y The annual tradition of stripping and dressing resumes at the Harkness Dance Festival, where the curator Doug Varone invites artists to ''strip down'' their work, sharing aspects of the process, before presenting a more polished, ''dressed'' product. This year those artists are Nora Chipaumire, casebolt and smith, Netta Yerushalmy, Kyle Abraham and David Dorfman, all makers of luscious, space-gobbling movement. Feb. 21-March 23. 92nd Street Y, 1395 Lexington Avenue, (212) 415-5500, 92y.org.
LUCIANA ACHUGAR For ''Otro Teatro,'' the darkly poetic Ms. Achugar began with the image of a collapsed theater. In a kind of ritualistic rebuilding, the Uruguayan choreographer probes beneath so-called civilized exteriors to expose more instinctual layers of society and self. The work has its world premiere at Walker Art Center (Feb. 27-March 1), then heads to New York Live Arts (April 2-5).
March
FLAMENCO FESTIVAL A large proscenium theater isn't ideal for flamenco, a form best experienced up close. For this annual City Center festival -- featuring Antonio Canales, Carlos Rodríguez, Karime Amaya and Jesús Carmona (March 6-7) and Eva Yerbabuena (March 8-9) -- get tickets early to snag seats at the front of the house. nycitycenter.org.
PAUL TAYLOR Six decades of making dances, and Mr. Taylor is still churning them out. His company's three-week Lincoln Center engagement includes a world premiere, a New York premiere and 19 other works from its ever-expanding repertory. March 11-30. (646) 214-5815, ptdc.org.
BETH GILL The title of Ms. Gill's new piece, ''New Work for the Desert,'' captures a certain tension in her work: her minimalist landscapes, while sometimes bleak, can also feel lush, regenerative, nourishing for the heart and mind. The cast here includes several dancers from her highly acclaimed ''Electric Midwife,'' plus a few newcomers. March 20-22. newyorklivearts.org.
MARTHA GRAHAM DANCE COMPANY The company continues its balancing act of preserving Graham's legacy while not getting stuck in the past. Its City Center season, while honoring the 70th anniversary of ''Appalachian Spring,'' also includes new works by Spain's esteemed Nacho Duato and the Greek choreographer Andonis Foniadakis. Let's hope that the expository speeches and video montages, a distracting part of last year's Joyce performances, are kept to a minimum. March 19-22. nycitycenter.org.
April
STEPHEN PETRONIO This year marks the big three-oh for Mr. Petronio's company. In addition to a revival of ''Strange Attractors, Part I'' from 1999, he's celebrating with two new, presumably contrasting works at the Joyce Theater: one to Philip Glass, the other to a commissioned score by the hip-hop producer Clams Casino. stephenpetronio.com.
REGGIE WILSON The best term for Mr. Wilson's bracing, rhythmically resonant works might be the one that often appears in his press materials: ''post-African/neo-HooDoo modern dances.'' In his latest, ''moses(es),'' he draws from Zora Neale Hurston's ''Moses, Man of the Mountains'' and his own travels to Egypt, Turkey and Mali to explore themes of leadership and migration. His Fist & Heel Performance Group takes the piece to the Dance Center of Columbia College Chicago (April 3-5), following its New York premiere at the Next Wave Festival (Dec. 4-7); colum.edu/dancecenter.
AMERICAN TAP DANCE FOUNDATION From its West Village headquarters, this organization directed by Tony Waag works hard to preserve tap's history and nurture fresh talent. Rhythm in Motion, its annual showcase of new dances by artists in residence, returns to the Theater at the 14th Street Y, April 8-13. 344 East 14th Street, (646) 230-9564, atdf.org.
WENDY WHELAN The New York City Ballet principal's admirable experiment in self-transformation, ''Restless Creature,'' comes to the Joyce Theater, April 2-6. Its spring tour also touches down in New Jersey; Chicago; Pittsburgh; Ann Arbor, Mich.; Boston; Louisville, Ky.; and Chapel Hill, N.C. Through April 11.
TRISHA BROWN DANCE COMPANY With the aging Ms. Brown no longer making new works, her dancers channel their energies into reconstructing older ones. The rarely seen ''Son of Gone Fishin' '' (1981) -- whose structure Ms. Brown has called '' the most rigorous of all my works'' -- should be a high point of the troupe's New York Live Arts season, which also includes ''Opal Loop/Cloud Installation #72503'' (1980), ''Solo Olos'' (1976) and ''Rogues'' (2011). newyorklivearts.org.
May
DANSE: A FRENCH-AMERICAN FESTIVAL OF PERFORMANCE AND IDEAS For this inaugural, citywide endeavor, the French Embassy partners with 14 presenters to host 17 United States premieres by a wide array of France-based artists, from Cecilia Bengolea and François Chaignaud at the Kitchen to Lyon Opera Ballet at the Brooklyn Academy of Music. Meanwhile, an editorial team will be chronicling it all, producing two publications to fulfill what the news release calls ''an increasing demand for critical analysis in the performance world.'' May 1-18. frenchculture.org.
FESTIVAL OF 21ST-CENTURY CHOREOGRAPHERS If you've ever found yourself feeling burnt out on Balanchine and Robbins, scouring the New York City Ballet calendar for dances made in this century, it's your lucky week. The company highlights its wealth of contemporary repertory -- by William Forsythe, Justin Peck, Alexei Ratmansky, Peter Martins, Liam Scarlett, Christopher Wheeldon and others -- with this much welcome festival (April 29-May 4). We'll see more of the fiercely talented Mr. Peck the following week, when his latest collaboration with Sufjan Stevens has its premiere at the spring gala (May 8). nycballet.org.
KENNEDY CENTER It's not often that musical ensembles commission dancers (the opposite is far more common), but in a rare reversal of roles, the National Symphony Orchestra has invited three companies -- Jessica Lang Dance, Keigwin + Company and New Ballet Ensemble, the troupe where the Memphis jookin virtuoso Lil Buck got his start -- to create works for ''New Moves: Symphony + Dance'' (May 7-17). Also this month: a visit from the Bolshoi Ballet (May 20-25), one of the many illustrious companies passing through the center this year. 2700 F Street, NW, Washington, (202) 467-4600, kennedy-center.org.
