HEADLINE: Oh, Romeo, Romeo, What a Season Ahead

BYLINE: By STEVEN McELROY

 BODY:
All dates are subject to change.
September
STOP. RESET. Those reading this in print will appreciate the timeliness of this plot: As e-books proliferate, the sales of printed ones fall, so the owner of the oldest African-American publishing company in Chicago needs to take stock, decide which employees are most relevant and determine how to survive in this new play, written and directed by Regina Taylor. She is one of the Residency Five playwrights working at Signature Theater Company in a five-year program to give each of them three premiere productions. In previews. Opens Sunday. Closes Sept. 29. Romulus Linney Courtyard Theater at Pershing Square Signature Center, 480 West 42nd Street, (212) 244-7529, signaturetheatre.org.
BRENDAN AT THE CHELSEA The Irish actor Adrian Dunbar stars as the Irish playwright Brendan Behan in a very New York story about Behan's years living in Manhattan at the Chelsea Hotel during the early 1960s (he died in 1964, at 41), when the hotel was a hub for bohemian artists. Mr. Dunbar also directs the five-character drama, written by Behan's niece Janet Behan, which comes to New York courtesy of the Lyric Theater of Belfast. In previews. Opens Sunday. Closes Oct. 6. The Acorn Theater at Theater Row, 410 West 42nd Street, Telecharge, brendanchelsea.com. 
FETCH CLAY, MAKE MAN New York Theater Workshop will open its season with Will Power's play about the unlikely friendship between the boxer Cassius Clay (later known as Muhammad Ali) and the comedian and film actor Stepin Fetchit, as both men try to control their legacies during the 1960s. The play, based on a true story, is directed by Des McAnuff. In previews. Opens Thursday. Closes Oct. 13. New York Theater Workshop, 79 East Fourth Street, Ticket Central, nytw.org.
MR. BURNS, A POST-ELECTRIC PLAY This imaginative play by Anne Washburn (''The Internationalist'') ''is the sort of once-in-a-blue-moon show that stays stuck in your brain long after it has chilled you to the bone,'' Peter Marks wrote in The Washington Post when the show had its premiere at the Woolly Mammoth Theater in Washington last year. The wacky premise here is that in a world where the electric grid has malfunctioned, a group of people commits one particular episode of ''The Simpsons'' to memory, and the story is told over the years. Naturally it unravels as in a game of telephone, highlighting the ever-changing nature, and needs, of pop culture across the generations. In previews. Opens next Sunday. Closes Oct. 6. Playwrights Horizons Mainstage Theater, 416 West 42nd Street, Ticket Central, playwrightshorizons.org.
WOMEN OR NOTHING Two women, a couple, very much want a child and are willing to bring a man into the picture in this new comedy, the first full-length play by Ethan Coen. Though better known as a filmmaker to many, he's known to Atlantic Theater Company attendees as a playwright too, but thus far only of one-acts. David Cromer directs. In previews. Opens Sept. 16. Closes Oct. 6. Linda Gross Theater, 336 West 20th Street, Theatermania, atlantictheater.org.
PHILIP GOES FORTH A young rebel leaves his father and the family business to move to New York and become a playwright -- a path traveled by many before and since this comedy by George Kelly played on Broadway in 1931. One obstacle for poor Philip, though, is that he can't write. In previews. Opens Sept. 22. Closes Oct. 20. Mint Theater, 311 West 43rd Street, Theatermania, minttheater.org.
ROMEO AND JULIET Shakespeare is always one of the most produced playwrights around town, but this season it seems that, even by those standards, he is having a moment. To start things off, Orlando Bloom and Condola Rashad will play the title characters in a Broadway production of this tale of doomed lovers. (Another high-profile version of the tragedy is coming from Classic Stage Company; see below.) The Montagues are white and the Capulet family black in this modern-set production, directed by David Leveaux and featuring a cast that also includes Brent Carver, Jayne Houdyshell, Chuck Cooper and Christian Camargo. In previews. Opens Sept. 19. Richard Rodgers Theater, 226 West 46th Street, Ticketmaster, romeoandjulietbroadway.com .
THE VELOCITY OF AUTUMN With the threat of a move to a nursing home looming, an artist locks herself in her home, prepared to blow up the place -- and the entire block, if needed. Estelle Parsons stars in this new play by Eric Coble, the opening production in what looks like a vibrant Arena Stage season. The show, which also features Stephen Spinella as her estranged son, had been announced for a Broadway run. A transfer is still a possibility, and the Arena Stage production is billed as pre-Broadway. Arena's artistic director, Molly Smith, is the director. In previews. Opens Sept. 19. Closes Oct. 20. Kreeger Theater at the Mead Center for American Theater, 1101 Sixth Street, SW, Washington, (202) 488-3300, arenastage.org.
THE GLASS MENAGERIE ''Memory is a force of gravity in this 'Glass Menagerie,' which is such a thorough rejuvenation of Tennessee Williams's 1944 drama that I hesitate to call it a revival,'' Ben Brantley wrote in The Times, referring to this production when staged at American Repertory Theater in Cambridge, Mass., this year. The same cast -- Cherry Jones as Amanda Wingfield; Zachary Quinto as her son, Tom; Celia Keenan-Bolger as the fragile Laura; and Brian J. Smith as Jim, the gentleman caller -- now comes to Broadway. Directed by John Tiffany, the show features movement by his frequent collaborator Steven Hoggett. In previews. Opens Sept. 26. Booth Theater, 222 West 45 Street, Telecharge, theglassmenageriebroadway.com .
BIG FISH Norbert Leo Butz stars as Edward Bloom, a father and a spinner of tall tales whose penchant for embellishment frustrates his son (Bobby Steggert) in this new musical, based on the 1998 novel by Daniel Wallace and the 2003 film directed by Tim Burton. The show, which was presented in Chicago last spring, is directed and choreographed by Susan Stroman, with music and lyrics by Andrew Lippa and a book by John August. Kate Baldwin co-stars as Bloom's wife, Sandra. In previews. Opens Oct. 6. Neil Simon Theater, 250 West 52nd Street, Ticketmaster, bigfishthemusical.com.
ARGUENDO A 1991 Supreme Court case about go-go dancers and public nudity is the subject of this latest concoction by Elevator Repair Service (Gatz''). Using actual court transcripts and parts of interviews with the justices, the inventive company, with the director John Collins, questions whether erotic dancing is art or immorality. Previews begin Tuesday. Opens Sept. 24. Closes Oct. 6. Public Theater, 425 Lafayette Street, at Astor Place, (212) 967-7555, publictheater.org.
THE FILM SOCIETY A teacher tries to keep the film club free of politics at a South African school for boys, but an apartheid controversy makes this impossible in this 1989 drama by Jon Robin Baitz. This opening production in the Keen Company season, about a man who ends up being torn between supporting a colleague and advancing his own career, will be directed by Jonathan Silverstein, Keen's artistic director. The cast includes Euan Morton and Roberta Maxwell. Previews begin Tuesday. Opens Oct. 1. Closes Oct. 26. Clurman Theater at Theater Row, 410 West 42nd Street, Telecharge, keencompany.org .
THE WHEEL A woman who claims to be averse to rearing children finds herself trying to return a young girl to her father in a surreal, war-torn world in this odd and intriguing drama by Zinnie Harris, which was presented in 2011 a the Edinburgh Festival Fringe in a National Theater of Scotland production. The production coming to the Steppenwolf Theater Company will star Joan Allen and is directed by Tina Landau. Previews begin Thursday. Opens Sept. 22. Closes Nov. 10. Downstairs Theater, 1650 North Halsted Street, Chicago, (312) 335-1650, steppenwolf.org.
ALL THE WAY The Emmy-winning actor Bryan Cranston (''Breaking Bad'') portrays Lyndon B. Johnson in this play by Robert Schenkkan (the Pulitzer-winning ''Kentucky Cycle'') about the suddenly appointed president's difficult first year in office. Brandon J. Dirden, Michael McKean and Reed Birney are among the rest of the cast, directed by Bill Rauch, in this American Repertory Theater production, which follows a world premiere at the Oregon Shakespeare Festival last year. Friday through Oct. 12. Loeb Drama Center, 64 Brattle Street, Cambridge, Mass., (617) 547-8300, americanrepertorytheater.org .
ON BORROWED TIME Long before Joel Grey won both Tony and Academy Awards for his portrayal of the M.C. in ''Cabaret,'' he took to the stage at the Cleveland Playhouse 1941, at age 9, to play Pud in this 1938 work by Paul Osborn. Now Mr. Grey will direct the play, about an orphaned boy and his grandfather who is able to cheat death, at Two River Theater, kicking off the company's 20th anniversary season. Previews begin on Saturday. Opens Sept. 20. Closes Oct. 13. Two River Theater, 21 Bridge Avenue, Redbank, N.J., (732) 345-1400, tworivertheater.org.
THE BLUE DRAGON The creative and versatile theater director Robert Lepage will present a sequel to his 1985 ''The Dragons' Trilogy'' as part of the annual Next Wave Festival at the Brooklyn Academy of Music (through Dec. 22). The new show, which Mr. Lepage developed along with his longtime collaborator, Marie Michaud, is about a Canadian artist living in China and facing issues of aging, creativity and the cultural differences between East to West. Mr. Lepage plays the artist, the same character he played in 1985. ''The Blue Dragon'' runs Sept. 18-21 at the Academy's Harvey Theater (651 Fulton Street, Fort Greene, Brooklyn). Next Wave includes dozens of other theater, music, opera and dance productions, including ''The Rime of the Ancient Mariner'' (Dec. 10-22, Harvey Theater), an adaptation of the Samuel Taylor Coleridge poem performed by Fiona Shaw with the dancer Daniel Hay-Gordon. Phyllida Lloyd directs. (718) 636-4100, bam.org .
BRONX BOMBERS Eric Simonson, who has written sports-themed shows about basketball (''Magic/Bird'') and football (''Lombardi'') in recent seasons, turns his attention to America's pastime in this new play about the New York Yankees, which he will also direct for Primary Stages. The cast includes Keith Nobbs as Billy Martin, Joe Pantoliano as Yogi Berra and Christopher Jackson as Derek Jeter. Sept. 17-Oct. 19. Duke on 42nd Street, 229 West 42nd Street, (646) 223-3010, primarystages.org .
NATURAL AFFECTION A young man returns home from a reform school stay to find that his single mother is living with a new man. Tensions in the disjointed family escalate and lead to brutality in this 1963 drama by William Inge, being revived by the Actors Company Theater, known as TACT. Kathryn Erbe and John Pankow are among the cast, directed by Jenn Thompson. Previews begin Sept. 17. Opens Sept. 26. Closes Oct. 26. Beckett Theater at Theater Row, 410 West 42nd Street, Telecharge, tactnyc.org.
BAD JEWS Joshua Harmon's comedy about a family squabble that digs into issues of faith gets remounted by the Roundabout Theater Company, following a well-received run at the company's Black Box Theater last fall. The four-member cast -- Tracee Chimo, Philip Ettinger, Molly Ranson and Michael Zegen -- will return, again under the direction of Daniel Aukin. Previews begin Sept. 19. Opens Oct. 3. Closes Dec. 15. Laura Pels Theater, 111 West 46th Street, (212) 719-1300, roundabouttheatre.org.
A NIGHT WITH JANIS JOPLIN It's a tough imitation to master, that screeching passion of the legendary singer whose songs -- ''Cry Baby'' among them -- will always be tied to the voice. But this concert-theater event, which arrives on Broadway after hitting several regional theaters (most recently, Arena Stage in Washington), boasts a lead performer who can come impressively close to the real deal. Mary Bridget Davies plays Joplin in a show written and directed by Randy Johnson. Previews begin Sept. 20. Opens Oct. 10. Lyceum Theater, 149 West 45th Street, Telecharge, anightwithjanisjoplin.com .
THE WINSLOW BOY When a 14-year old boy (Spencer Davis Milford) is accused of stealing, he is expelled from military school and his family pulls together in a costly fight to save their honor in this 1946 play by Terence Rattigan. The director Lindsay Posner brings his successful production from the Old Vic to Broadway with a new cast, including Mr. Milford, Zachary Booth, Michael Cumpsty, Mary Elizabeth Mastrantonio, Alessandro Nivola and Roger Rees. Previews begin Sept. 20. Opens Oct. 17. Closes Dec. 1. American Airlines Theater, 227 West 42nd Street, (212) 719-1300, roundabouttheatre.org.
TAMAR OF THE RIVER A young woman bravely pursues peace in a mythical, war-ravaged world in this new musical being presented by the Prospect Theater Company (''Working -- A Musical''). The book is by Marisa Michelson and Joshua H. Cohen, with music by Ms. Michelson and lyrics by Mr. Cohen. Daniel Goldstein (''Godspell'') is the director. Sept. 21-Oct. 20. Baruch Performing Arts Center, 55 Lexington Avenue; Theatermania, prospecttheater.org.
BEAUTIFUL -- THE CAROLE KING MUSICAL The story of the transformation of a Brooklyn kid, Carol Klein, into the successful singer-songwriter -- who, incidentally, wrote ''Will You Still Love Me Tomorrow'' when she was only 17 -- is told in a musical, naturally. This show, starring Jessie Mueller as Ms. King, features songs by Ms. King and Gerry Goffin, who was her husband from 1959 to 1968, as well as other songs of the period. The book is by Douglas McGrath and direction by Marc Bruni. A Broadway bow is planned after this West Coast premiere, with previews beginning Nov. 21 at the Stephen Sondheim Theater. But first, San Francisco: Sept. 24-Oct. 20, SHN Curran Theater, 445 Geary Street, (888) 746-1799, shnsf.com and beautifulonbroadway.com.
BIKE AMERICA Penny dumps her boyfriend in Boston and heads to California, on a bike, and encounters many characters in her quest to find some meaning in her life in this new play by Mike Lew (''microcrisis''), presented by Ma-Yi Theater Company. Moritz von Stuelpnagel (''Hand to God'') directs. Previews begin Sept. 24. Opens Oct. 1. Closes Oct. 20. Theater at St. Clement's, 423 West 46th Street, Theatermania, mayitheatre.org.
THE SUNSHINE BOYS Danny DeVito's physical weirdness and comic fearlessness make him well-suited to the role of an aging vaudeville comedian in this 1972 Neil Simon comedy, which was well-received at the Savoy Theater in London last year. In the American version, Judd Hirsch will play the other half of a comedy team that's been on nonspeaking terms for a decade. Richard Griffiths, who played the Hirsch role in London, died last spring. Fortunately, Mr. DeVito and his new co-star have teamed up quite successfully in the past, in the television series ''Taxi.'' Thea Sharrock directs. Previews begin Sept. 24. Opens Oct. 2. Closes Nov. 3. Ahmanson Theater at the Music Center, 135 North Grand Avenue, Los Angeles, (213) 972-4400, centertheatregroup.org.
SARAH FLOOD IN SALEM MASS The playwright Adriano Shaplin (''Sophie Gets the Horns''), the artistic director and co-founder of the Riot Group, reimagines the events leading up to the Salem Witch Trials in this new play, to have its premiere at the Flea Theater. Rebecca Wright will direct a cast that features members of the Bats, the company's resident acting company. Previews begin Sept. 24. Opens Oct. 6. Closes Oct. 27. 41 White Street, near Church Street, Theatermania, theflea.org.
I CAME TO LOOK FOR YOU ON TUESDAY This new drama by Chiori Miyagawa (I Have Been to Hiroshima Mon Amour'') tells the stories of 20 characters who have lost something in the wake of natural disaster, centering on Maia, whose mother's sacrifice during a tsunami allowed her to survive. The show, directed by Alice Reagan, is one of a slew of offerings at the downtown mainstay La MaMa this fall, including a solo series, the Queer NY International Arts Festival (Oct. 25-Nov. 3), and adaptations of both Virginia Woolf's ''Room of One's Own'' and ''La Vie Matérielle'' by Marguerite Duras, directed by Irina Brook (''Waiting for the Dream''). ''I Came to Look for You on Tuesday'' is part of a the Tuesday Project, which will also include art installations and other events around the city related to the aftermath of war. Sept. 26-Oct. 13. First Floor Theater, 74 East Fourth Street, (212) 475-7710, lamama.org.
NIGHTMARE: KILLERS2 Charles Manson and other famous killers will haunt the twisty halls of this immersive haunted house in the 10th anniversary installment of the ''Nightmare'' experience, which offers a fresh take each time. Last year's serial killer haunt was apparently popular -- and horrifying -- enough to prompt a sequel, with new killers both historical and fictional. Sept. 26-Nov. 2. 107 Suffolk Street, near Rivington Street, hauntedhousenyc.com .
ROMEO AND JULIET There will be dual Montague-Capulet duels going on in New York this fall, as Classic Stage Company presents an Off Broadway version of this Shakespeare tragedy of young love gone off the rails, at the same time a Broadway revival is on the boards (see above). In this Off Broadway version, Elizabeth Olsen is Juliet and Julian Cihi her Romeo, with T. R. Knight as Mercutio and Daphne Rubin-Vega as the Nurse. Tea Alagiae directs. Previews begin Sept. 27. Opens Oct. 16. Closes Nov. 3. Classic Stage Company, 136 East 13th Street, Theatermania, classicstage.org.
THE FEW A woman tries to keep her small-town Idaho newspaper afloat by running personal ads for love-starved truckers in this new play by Samuel D. Hunter (''The Whale''), which will have its world premiere at the Old Globe (and a New York production via Rattlestick Playwrights Theater next spring). Davis McCallum directs this comedy about the need for human connection. Previews begin Sept. 28. Scheduled to open Oct. 3. Closes Oct. 27. Old Globe, 1363 Old Globe Way, San Diego, (619) 234-5623, theoldglobe.org.
A TIME TO KILL John Grisham's Mississippi-set novel about a white lawyer defending a black man who took justice into his own hands was turned into a 1996 movie, but now a Grisham novel is being adapted to the stage for the first time. If all goes well, he's got more than 20 others, including his latest book, ''Sycamore Row,'' which features the hero of ''A Time to Kill'' in another courtroom battle three years later. The new play, adapted by Rupert Holmes and directed by Ethan McSweeny, features Sebastian Arcelus as the lawyer and John Douglas Thompson as his client. Previews begin Sept. 28. Opens Oct. 20. John Golden Theater, 252 West 45th Street, Telecharge, atimetokillonbroadway.com.
FUN HOME Alison Bechdel's autobiographical graphic novel ''Fun Home,'' about her childhood and her relationship with her father -- who died suddenly -- gets the musical treatment in this new show with a book and lyrics by Lisa Kron (''Well'') and music by Jeanine Tesori (''Caroline, or Change''). Sam Gold directs. Previews begin Sept. 30. Opens Oct. 22. Closes Nov. 3. Public Theater, 425 Lafayette Street, at Astor Place, (212) 967-7555, publictheater.org.
October
THE SNOW GEESE One of a small number of new plays coming to Broadway this fall, this drama by Sharr White (''The Other Place''), set during World War I, stars Mary-Louise Parker as a newly widowed mother trying to keep her family, and her finances, together. Daniel Sullivan directs the co-production of Manhattan Theater Club and MCC Theater. Previews begin Oct. 1. Opens Oct. 24. Samuel J. Friedman Theater, 261 West 47th Street, Telecharge, manhattantheatreclub.org.
BETRAYAL The married couple Daniel Craig (''A Steady Rain'') and Rachel Weisz will portray another married couple in this revival of Harold Pinter's bleak 1978 drama about infidelity, one of two Pinter plays on Broadway this fall (see ''No Man's Land,'' below). Ms. Weisz is making her Broadway debut, as is the English actor Rafe Spall, who plays the best friend of Robert, Mr. Craig's character, and who is having an affair with Robert's wife. Mike Nichols directs the three-member cast, and tickets are already getting scarce. Previews begin Oct. 1. Opens Oct. 27. Closes Jan. 5. Barrymore Theater, 243 West 47th Street, Telecharge, betrayalbroadway.com.
DISPLACED HINDU GODS The Hindu gods that represent creation (Brahma), preservation (Vishnu) and destruction (Shiva) are placed in the contemporary Western world in this trilogy of new plays by Aditi Brennan Kapil. The three plays -- ''Brahman/i: A One-Hijra Stand-Up Comedy Show,'' ''The Chronicles of Kalki'' and ''Shiv'' -- are presented in three different styles; stand-up comedy, girl-gang thriller and sci-fi fantasy. Previews begin Oct. 2. Opens Oct. 5. Closes Oct. 27. Mixed Blood Theater, 1501 South Fourth Street, Minneapolis, (612) 338-6131, mixedblood.com.
THE SEAGULL Culture Project presents an adaptation of Chekhov's play about an aging actress and the frustrated artists around her, transplanting the story to late-19th-century Ireland. Max Stafford-Clark directs the adaptation by Thomas Kilroy, with a cast including Trudie Styler (Sting's wife), Alan Cox and Amanda Quaid. Previews begin Oct. 3. Scheduled to open Oct. 13. Closes Nov. 3. Lynn Redgrave Theater at Culture Project, 45 Bleecker Street, Theatermania, cultureproject.org.
THE LANDING Three thematically connected stories form the basis of this new musical by the perhaps unlikely team of the young book and lyrics writer Greg Pierce (''Slowgirl'') and the veteran composer John Kander. Walter Bobbie will direct a cast that includes David Hyde Pierce, Julia Murney and Paul Anthony Stewart. Previews begin Oct. 3. Opens Oct. 23. Closes Nov. 10. Vineyard Theater, 108 East 15th Street, (212) 353-0303, vineyardtheatre.org.
JULIUS CAESAR The director Phyllida Lloyd's (''Mary Stuart'') all female, rock 'n' roll-infused production of Shakespeare's political tragedy comes from the Donmar Warehouse in London to St. Ann's Warehouse in Brooklyn. The London production was well-received and will arrive in New York with most of its cast intact and with the same play-within-a-play concept -- inmates and guards at a women's prison are performing the Shakespeare play. Oct. 3-Nov. 3. St. Ann's Warehouse, 29 Jay Street, Brooklyn, (718) 254-8779, stannswarehouse.org.
GRASSES OF A THOUSAND COLORS A doctor finds an unusual if somewhat revolting way to battle world hunger in this play by Wallace Shawn, who is collaborating with the director André Gregory again soon after the summer run of ''The Designated Mourner.'' A co-production of the Public Theater and Theater for a New Audience, ''Grasses'' features Julie Hagerty, Emily Cass McDonnell, Mr. Shawn and Jennifer Tilly. Previews begin Oct. 7. Opens Oct. 28. Closes Nov. 10. Public Theater, 425 Lafayette Street, near Astor Place, (212) 967-7555, publictheater.org.
ROOSEVELVIS Ann, a slaughterhouse worker, comes to the realization that she is not entirely female or male and sets off on a surreal road trip with her lover, to Graceland from the Badlands, as they encounter the America of the moment -- while transitioning into Teddy Roosevelt and Elvis. How's that for an inventive plot? The Brooklyn company the Team (''Mission Drift'') has plenty of 'em. The company's latest was co-commissioned by Bushwick Starr in Brooklyn and the Gate and Almeida Theaters in London. Oct. 8-Nov. 2. Bushwick Starr, 207 Starr Street, Brooklyn, Theatermania, theteamplays.org.
JUNO AND THE PAYCOCK The Irish Repertory Theater will present Sean O'Casey's 1924 drama about the dashed hopes of a poor Dublin family during the Irish Civil War of the 1920s, with the theater's artistic director, Charlotte Moore, directing. The cast includes J. Smith-Cameron, Ciaran O'Reilly and John Keating. Previews begin Oct. 9. Opens Oct. 20. Closes Dec. 8. Francis J. Greenburger Mainstage, 132 West 22nd Street, (212) 727-2737, irishrep.org.
EAGER TO LOSE The burlesque performer Tansy stars in this new show set in the fictitious Tim Tam Room, where the club's star (conveniently named Tansy) announces that she is quitting, throwing the M.C. for a loop. The show, written by Matthew-Lee Erlbach (''Handbook for an American Revolutionary''), was developed through the ANT Fest and Project Residency programs at Ars Nova. Previews begin Oct. 9. Opens Oct. 22. Closes Nov. 2. Ars Nova, 511 West 54th Street, Theatermania, arsnovanyc.com.
THE TALLEST TREE IN THE FOREST The singer, actor and outspoken human rights advocate Paul Robeson is the subject of this new solo show, written and performed by Daniel Beaty (Emergence-see!'') and directed by Moisés Kaufman. The show is a co-production among several theaters, is now running at Kansas City Repertory Theater (through Sept. 28) and will be presented at Arena Stage in Washington from Jan. 10 to Feb. 16. In La Jolla: Previews begin Oct. 10. Scheduled to open Oct. 12. Closes Nov. 3. Sheila and Hughes Potiker Theater, La Jolla Playhouse, 2910 La Jolla Village Drive, San Diego, (858) 550-1010, lajollaplayhouse.org.
DOMESTICATED A married couple (Jeff Goldblum and Laurie Metcalf) become enmeshed in a scandal in this new play by the Pulitzer Prize- and Tony Award-winning playwright Bruce Norris (''Clybourne Park''). Anna D. Shapiro (''August: Osage County'') will direct the Lincoln Center Theater commission. Previews begin Oct. 10. Opens Nov. 4. Mitzi E. Newhouse Theater, 150 West 65th Street, lct.org.
THE DOWNTOWN LOOP 3-Legged Dog Productions puts its extensive video capabilities to use in this new show by Ben Gassman. Audiences will take a virtual guided bus tour through the streets of Manhattan in the production, directed by Meghan Finn, which explores gentrification and economic revitalization. Oct. 15-Nov. 17. 3LD Art & Technology Center, 80 Greenwich Street, 3ldnyc.org.
LITTLE MISS SUNSHINE Hopeful little Olive Hoover (Hannah Rose Nordberg) and her eccentric family set out on a cross-country trek on a quest for Olive to win the preteen Little Miss Sunshine pageant in this new musical comedy based on the 2006 film written by Michael Arndt. Stephanie J. Block, Will Swenson and Rory O'Malley are among the rest of the cast of this Second Stage Theater production. The book and direction are by James Lapine and the music and lyrics by William Finn. Previews begin Oct. 15. Opens Nov. 14. Closes Dec. 8. Second Stage's Tony Kiser Theater, 305 West 43rd Street, (212) 246-4422, 2st.com.
TWELFTH NIGHT and RICHARD III The two-time Tony Award-winning actor Mark Rylance (''Jerusalem,'' ''Boeing-Boeing'') -- who likes to give bizarre Tony acceptance speeches -- will offer what Shakespeare fans must surely consider a golden opportunity: to see him in two classic roles in a rotating repertory on Broadway. (Though usually rare, this is one of two repertory events coming to Broadway; see ''No Man's Land'' and ''Waiting for Godot,'' below.) Mr. Rylance will play a woman, the grieving Countess Olivia, in ''Twelfth Night'' and the title character in the history play. Stephen Fry, making his Broadway debut, is among the rest of the company in these productions, directed by Tim Carroll, which ran in London last fall. Previews begin Oct. 15. Opens Nov. 10. Belasco Theater, 111 West 44th Street, Telecharge, shakespearebroadway.com.
ONE NIGHT ... The Pulitzer Prize-winning playwright Charles Fuller (''A Soldier's Story'') turns to a military theme again in his new drama about women in the armed forces and the price of serving one's country. Clinton Turner Davis directs the co-production of the Cherry Lane Theater and Rattlestick Playwrights Theater. Previews begin Oct. 17. Opens Oct. 30. Closes Nov. 24. Cherry Lane Theater, 38 Commerce Street, Theatermania, cherrylanetheatre.org.
AFTER MIDNIGHT The 17-member Jazz at Lincoln Center All-Stars, led by Wynton Marsalis, feature prominently in this reinvention of an evening at the Cotton Club, the famous Harlem nightclub where Duke Ellington, Cab Calloway, Count Basie and a slew of other African-American performers wowed the crowds in the 1920s and '30s. The show, which was presented as ''Cotton Club Parade'' at City Center in 2011 (and briefly reprised in 2012), is directed and choreographed by Warren Carlyle and will feature Dulé Hill (last on Broadway in ''Stick Fly'') as the host and Fantasia Barrino as the first of several planned guest stars in limited engagements. Previews begin Oct. 18. Opens Nov. 3. Brooks Atkinson Theater, 256 West 47th Street, Ticketmaster, aftermidnightbroadway.com.
A MIDSUMMER NIGHT'S DREAM Julie Taymor's visually imaginative direction ought to turn one of Shakespeare's most produced comedies into something seemingly new in this production, the first for Theater for a New Audience at the company's new Brooklyn home. Previews begin Oct. 19. Opens Nov. 2. Closes Jan. 12. Theater for a New Audience, 262 Ashland Place, Brooklyn, (212) 229-2819, tfana.org.
THE COMMONS OF PENSACOLA Blythe Danner and Sarah Jessica Parker will portray a mother and daughter grappling with the aftermath of a Wall Street scam perpetuated by their husband and father. The play marks the playwriting debut of the actress Amanda Peet and will be directed by Lynne Meadow for Manhattan Theater Club. Previews begin Oct. 22. Opens Nov. 21. Closes Jan. 5. City Center Stage I, 131 West 55th Street, (212) 581-1212, manhattantheatreclub.org.
A GENTLEMAN'S GUIDE TO LOVE AND MURDER The chameleonlike actor Jefferson Mays plays multiple roles -- and dies multiple times -- in this musical comedy that comes to Broadway after critically lauded runs at Hartford Stage and the Old Globe. The story, about a frustrated Edwardian man (Bryce Pinkham) separated from a family earldom by eight living relations, features Mr. Mays (known for being similarly adaptable in ''I Am My Own Wife'') as all of those relations. The book is by Robert L. Freedman, music by Steven Lutvak and lyrics by both men, and the show is directed by Darko Tresnjak. Previews begin Oct. 22. Opens Nov. 17. Walter Kerr Theater, 219 West 48th Street, Telecharge, agentlemansguidebroadway.com.
THE APPLE FAMILY PLAYS: SCENES FROM LIFE IN THE COUNTRY The final play in Richard Nelson's four-play series about the liberal Apple family, ''Regular Singing,'' will be presented this fall in rotation with revivals of his previous Apple plays. Each of the earlier works opened on the nights they were set: ''That Hopey Changey Thing'' took place -- and opened -- on election night, 2010; ''Sweet and Sad'' on the 10th anniversary of the Sept. 11 attacks; and ''Sorry'' on election night, 2012. The newest Apple play takes place, and opens, on Nov. 22, the 50th anniversary of the assassination of President John F. Kennedy. Previews begin Oct. 22. Opens Nov. 22. Closes Dec. 15. Public Theater, 425 Lafayette Street, at Astor Place, (212) 967-7555, publictheater.org.
MONA'S HOUSE OF DANCE Personal fears and desires come to the fore as students prepare for a competition in a small-town tap dance studio in this new collaboration between two unorthodox companies: Richard Maxwell's New York City Players (''Neutral Hero'') and Tina Satter's company Half Straddle (''Seagull (Thinking of you)''). Ms. Satter is the writer and director of the show, which will be presented in a dance studio at Abrons Arts Center. Previews begin Oct. 23. Opens Oct. 27. Closes Nov. 9. Abrons Arts Center, 466 Grand Street, Theatermania, abronsartscenter.org.
MACBETH With Alan Cumming's (nearly) one-man adaptation of Shakespeare's bloody Scottish tragedy still fresh in our minds, here comes a version in which the title character and his doomed Lady Macbeth will not be played by the same performer: Ethan Hawke and Anne-Marie Duff take on the blood-soaked married couple. Jack O'Brien directs the Lincoln Center Theater production. Previews begin Oct. 24. Opens Nov. 21. Vivian Beaumont Theater, 150 West 65 Street, Telecharge, lct.org.
THE JACKSONIAN His marriage crumbling, a dentist (Ed Harris) is booted from home by his wife (Amy Madigan) and takes up residence in a creepy hotel in this dark tale by Beth Henley (''Crimes of the Heart''), set in the racially divided Jackson, Miss., of 1964. Glenne Headly and Bill Pullman are also in the cast of this New Group production, directed by Robert Falls, that follows the premiere last year at the Geffen Playhouse in Los Angeles. Previews begin Oct. 25. Opens Nov. 7. Closes Dec. 15. New Group at the Acorn Theater, Theater Row, 410 West 42nd Street, Telecharge, thenewgroup.org.
NO MAN'S LAND and WAITING FOR GODOT Ian McKellen, Patrick Stewart, Billy Crudup and Shuler Hensley will perform together in Harold Pinter's enigmatic comedy ''No Man's Land'' and Samuel Beckett's existentialist tragicomedy, which play an alternating schedule -- even allowing theatergoers to completely scramble their brains by seeing both shows in the same day on select dates. Sean Mathias directs. Previews begin Oct. 26. Opens Nov. 24. Closes Feb. 1. Cort Theater, 138 West 48th Street, Telecharge, twoplaysinrep.com.
SMALL ENGINE REPAIR Former high school buddies Frank, Swaino and Packie gather years later in Frank's repair shop, where old wounds and new mysteries come up in this psychological thriller by John Pollono. The play was produced by Rogue Machine Theater Los Angeles in 2011, and now Jo Bonney directs the New York premiere for MCC Theater. Previews begin Oct. 30. Opens Nov. 20. Closes Dec. 8. MCC Theater at the Lucille Lortel Theater, 121 Christopher Street, Theatermania, mcctheater.org.
November
THE MUTILATED Two utterly singular performers, Mink Stole and Penny Arcade, will star in Tennessee Williams's strange late-career play about Trinket and Celeste -- two women, an oil heiress and an aging prostitute, whose friendship is almost destroyed when one reveals a secret about the other. Following an appearance at the annual Provincetown Tennessee Williams Theater Festival (Sept. 26-29), the production, directed by Cosmin Chivu, comes to New York City. Previews begin Nov. 1. Opens Nov. 10. Closes Nov. 24. New Ohio Theater, 154 Christopher Street, (888) 596-1027, newohiotheatre.org.
APPROPRIATE A disturbing memento is discovered among the belongings left behind by a recently deceased patriarch, stirring up the Arkansas family he has left behind, in this play by Branden Jacobs-Jenkins (''Neighbors''), which was presented at the Humana Festival lastspring and will also take a bow at Signature Theater Company, where Mr. Jacobs-Jenkins is in a residency, early next year. This fall, though, Liesl Tommy directs the show for the Woolly Mammoth Theater Company. Nov. 4-Dec. 1. 641 D Street NW, Washington, (202) 393-3939, woollymammoth.net.
ALL THAT FALL Trevor Nunn's stage adaptation of this haunting Samuel Beckett radio play, starring Eileen Atkins and Michael Gambon, was well-received in London last year. In the seemingly simple plot, a woman goes to meet her blind husband at a train station. She encounters a number of people along the way, and her husband tells a disturbing tale on the way home, in what turns out to be a typically Beckettian meditation on death. Nov. 5-Dec. 8. 59E59 Theaters, 59 East 59th Street, Ticket Central, 59e59.org.
IF/THEN About to turn 40, Elizabeth (Idina Menzel) seeks a fresh start in New York City, where the possibilities seem limitless, in this new musical by the team behind the Pulitzer-winning ''Next to Normal'' (music by Tom Kitt, book and lyrics by Brian Yorkey and direction by Michael Greif). LaChanze (''The Color Purple'') and Anthony Rapp are among the rest of the cast. A Broadway production of ''If/Then'' is planned for the spring, with previews beginning at the Richard Rodgers Theater on March 4 for a March 27 opening, but first an out-of-town tryout. Yes, some productions still do those. Nov. 5-Dec. 8. The National Theater, 1321 Pennsylvania Avenue, Washington, Etix, ifthenthemusical.com.
700 SUNDAYS Billy Crystal's autobiographical play, which won a Tony Award for special theatrical event after opening on Broadway in 2004, is back for a limited run. Mr. Crystal, who wrote the script with some contributions from Alan Zweibel, has presented ''700 Sundays'' in many other sites over the years, and press materials say this will be the final engagement. Des McAnuff directs. Previews begin Nov. 5. Opens Nov. 13. Closes Jan. 5. Imperial Theater, 249 West 45th Street, Telecharge, 700sundaysonbroadway.com.
RADIO CITY CHRISTMAS SPECTACULAR Like the tree at Rockefeller Center and the decorated store windows along Fifth Avenue, this annual holiday extravaganza with the high-kicking Rockettes is New York holiday traditional at its finest. Nov. 8-Dec. 30. Radio City Music Hall, Ticketmaster, radiocitychristmas.com.
AND AWAY WE GO This new backstage comedy by Terrence McNally was to have its premiere at the Pearl Theater Company last spring, but that was delayed as the theater worked through some financial problems. This fall the time-jumping play, about the many challenges facing theater companies from ancient Athens to the present, is happily back on the company's schedule. Jack Cummings III, artistic director of Transport Group, will direct. Previews begin Nov. 12. Opens Nov. 24. Closes Dec. 15. Pearl Theater, 555 West 42nd Street, (212) 563-9261, pearltheatre.org.
FAMILY FURNITURE The Flea Theater presents a new play by A. R. Gurney, a coming-of-age tale set in the 1950s, directed by Thomas Kail (''In the Heights''). Previews begin Nov. 12. Opens Nov. 24. Closes Dec. 22. 41 White Street, Theatermania, theflea.org.
THE (CURIOUS CASE OF THE) WATSON INTELLIGENCE Remember when that computer Watson competed on the game show ''Jeopardy!'' in 2011? The playwright Madeleine George (''The Zero Hour'') sure does. Leigh Silverman will direct this premiere for Playwrights Horizons, a time-hopping tribute to several Watsons, including the computer, the Sherlock Holmes sidekick, the engineer who built Alexander Graham Bell's first telephone and a modern-day tech-geek searching for love. Previews begin Nov. 17. Opens Dec. 9. Closes Dec. 29. Playwrights Horizons Mainstage Theater, 416 West 42nd Street, Ticket Central, playwrightshorizons.org.
December
THE LIFE AND DEATH OF MARINA ABRAMOVIC The visionary director Robert Wilson's look at the life of the performance artist Marina Abramovic stars Ms. Abramovic, Willem Dafoe and the singer Antony in a production that was commissioned by the Manchester International Festival and Teatro Real, Madrid with Theater Basel, Art Basel, Holland Festival, Salford City Council, and deSingel, Antwerp. Dec. 12-21. Park Avenue Armory, 643 Park Avenue, (212) 616-3930, armoryonpark.org.
MACHINAL Rebecca Hall will star in the first Broadway revival of this drama by Sophie Treadwell since its 1928 premiere. The play, about a woman living a stifled, boring existence in the male-dominated 1920s who takes extreme actions to break out, is directed by Lyndsey Turner. Previews begin Dec. 20. Opens Jan. 16. Closes March 2. American Airlines Theater, 227 West 42nd Street, (212) 719-1300, roundabouttheatre.org.
January
OUTSIDE MULLINGAR Brian F. O'Byrne (''Frozen'') and Debra Messing (of ''Smash'' and ''Will & Grace'' fame, and in her Broadway debut) will star in this world premiere romantic comedy by John Patrick Shanley (''Doubt'') about two painfully shy souls living next door to each other in rural Ireland and watching the years slowly roll by as they fear they will lose their chance for romance. Doug Hughes directs. Previews begin Jan. 2. Opens Jan. 23. Samuel J. Friedman Theater, 261 West 47th Street, Telecharge, manhattantheatreclub.org.
KING LEAR Frank Langella plays the monarch slowly falling from grace in this Chichester Festival Theater production of Shakespeare's monumental tragedy, directed by Angus Jackson. The show comes to the Brooklyn Academy of Music after a fall run at the Minerva Theater in Chichester (Oct. 31-Nov. 30). Jan. 7-Feb. 9, Harvey Theater, 651 Fulton Street, Brooklyn, (718) 636-4100, bam.org.
THE LONELINESS OF THE LONG DISTANCE RUNNER Alan Sillitoe's 1959 short story about a teenager in a prison school who turns to long-distance running as an escape, is adapted for the stage and moved to the 21st century by the British playwright Roy Williams. Leah C. Gardiner directs the Atlantic Theater Company production. Previews begin Jan. 8. Opens Jan. 21. Atlantic Stage 2, 330 West 16th Street, Ticket Central, atlantictheater.org.
THE BRIDGES OF MADISON COUNTY Robert James Waller's best-selling 1992 novel was already turned into a (not entirely well-reviewed) 1995 film starring Meryl Streep and Clint Eastwood, and now Marsha Norman (book) and Jason Robert Brown (music and lyrics) have transformed the romantic tale into a Broadway musical. Kelli O'Hara stars as an Italian woman turned Iowa housewife whose chance meeting with a National Geographic photographer (Steven Pasquale) incites her passion and forces her to choose between love and security. The show, which had its premiere at the Williamstown Theater Festival lastmonth, is directed by Bartlett Sher. Previews begin Jan. 13. Opens Feb. 27. Gerald Schoenfeld Theater, 236 West 45th Street, Telecharge, bridgesofmadisoncountymusical.com.
LUNA GALE When a social worker takes on the case of two drug-addicted teenagers accused of neglecting their infant child, she opens a can of worms and becomes mired in the conflicts of a family with a secretive past in this new drama by Rebecca Gilman (''Spinning Into Butter''). Robert Falls will direct the premiere in Chicago for the Goodman Theater, where Ms. Gilman is an artistic associate. Jan. 18-Feb. 23. Albert Theater at the Goodman, 170 North Dearborn Street, Chicago, (312) 443-3800, goodmantheatre.org.
February
TALES FROM RED VIENNA A woman widowed thanks to World War I finds an illicit way to survive, but she struggles to keep separate her public and private identities in this new play by David Grimm (''Measure for Pleasure''), directed by Kate Whoriskey (''Ruined'') for Manhattan Theater Club. Previews begin Feb. 26. Opens March 18. Closes April 20. City Center Stage I, 131 West 55th Street, (212) 581-1212, manhattantheatreclub.org.
LITTLE ME The annual Encores! series of too-short runs of seldom-performed musicals will start with Neil Simon's first musical, about obsession with celebrity status, that might feel just as timely today as it did in 1962. Christian Borle (''Smash'' and ''Peter and the Starcatcher'') stars in seven roles as the lovers of the main character in this show, directed by John Rando, which is the first in the Encores! season that will also include a special event this fall, ''A BED AND A CHAIR: A New York Love Affair'' (Nov. 13-17), to feature Stephen Sondheim's music arranged and performed by the Jazz at Lincoln Center Orchestra with Wynton Marsalis and directed by John Doyle and two other regular Encores! season shows, ''The Most Happy Fella'' (April 2-6) and ''Irma La Douce'' (May 7-11). The ''Little Me'' music is by Cy Coleman and the lyrics by Carolyn Leigh. Feb. 5-9. City Center, 131 West 55th Street, (212) 581-1212, nycitycenter.org.
ROCKY The unknown Philadelphia boxer Rocky Balboa gets his shot at the big time in this new musical based on the romantic Oscar-winning 1976 film. The show, with a book by Thomas Meehan, lyrics by Lynn Ahrens and music by Stephen Flaherty, had its premiere in Hamburg, Germany, in late 2012 and was well-received there. The powerful creative team also includes the director Alex Timbers (''Bloody Bloody Andrew Jackson''), the choreographer Kelly Devine and Steven Hoggett -- a guy who knows his way around the boxing ring -- handling fight choreography. Previews begin in February with an opening planned for March. Winter Garden Theater, 1634 Broadway, rockybroadway.com.
March
LES MISÉRABLES I dreamed a dream that this show was on Broadway not long ago. Oh wait, that was real. When this French Revolution epic was revived in 2006 (after a long run from 1987-2003), there was a palpable ''again?'' feeling among some theatergoers. So the producer Cameron Mackintosh will have that to contend with that as the show, based on Victor Hugo's novel of the same name, makes its way to Broadway once again. The current version has apparently been reimagined, and the cast has yet to be announced. Previews begin March 1. Opens March 23. Imperial Theater, 249 West 45th Street, Telecharge, lesmiz.com.
A RAISIN IN THE SUN Diahann Carroll will return to Broadway after three decades to play Lena Younger, the beleaguered matriarch of a struggling black family in 1950s Chicago in this drama by Lorraine Hansberry. Denzel Washington plays her frustrated son, Walter Lee, and the cast, directed by Kenny Leon, also includes Sophie Okonedo (''Hotel Rwanda''), Anika Noni Rose (''Caroline, or Change''), Stephen Tyrone Williams (''Lucky Guy'') and Jason Dirden and Stephen McKinley Henderson (both ''Fences''). Previews begin March 8. Opens April 3. Closes June 15. Barrymore Theater, 243 West 47th Street, Telecharge.
BULLETS OVER BROADWAY If a new playwright wants to have his work on Broadway, he'd better cast a mobster's talent-free gal in a key role or fuggedaboutit. That's the premise of the 1920s-set Woody Allen movie ''Bullets Over Broadway'' (1994), in any case. (Today it seems more likely that playwrights need to adapt film scripts rather than write from scratch.) This new musical is to feature Zach Braff as the playwright, Vincent Pastore as the mobster and Hélene Yorke as his girlfriend. Happily, Mr. Allen is doing the adaptation himself, and Susan Stroman will direct and choreograph the show, using music from the 1920s. Previews begin March 11. Opens April 10. St. James Theater, 246 West 44th Street, Telecharge, bulletsoverbroadway.com.
THESE PAPER BULLETS Yale Repertory Theater has sure pulled together an impressive creative team for what sounds like an interesting new musical: A rock 'n' update of Shakespeare's ''Much Ado About Nothing,'' with a Beatles-like band at its core. The writer Rolin Jones (''The Intelligent Design of Jenny Chow'') is adapting the script, and Green Day's Billie Joe Armstrong (''American Idiot'') is writing the songs. Jackson Gay (also ''Jenny Chow'') directs. Previews begin March 14. Opens March 20. Closes April 5. University Theater, 222 York Street, New Haven, (203) 432-1234, yalerep.org.
ACT ONE James Lapine is both the writer and director of this adaptation of the autobiography by Moss Hart, the playwright and director who famously teamed up with George S. Kaufman on several comedies, including ''The Man Who Came to Dinner'' and ''You Can't Take It With You.'' The Lincoln Center Theater production will begin previews March 20. Opens April 17. Vivian Beaumont Theater, 150 West 65 Street, Telecharge, lct.org.
HEDWIG AND THE ANGRY INCH A Broadway production of this John Cameron Mitchell/Stephen Trask rock musical that started out Off Off Broadway in the 1990s was rumored awhile back, but now it is apparently on track for the spring. The show, about a decidedly nonfamous German singer whose ''sex-change operation got botched,'' has its fans, so don't let us down! Neil Patrick Harris has signed on for the lead role and seems like a good casting choice, and the director Michael Mayer, who clearly knows his way around a rock musical (see ''American Idiot'' and ''Spring Awakening''), is also on board. Previews are planned for March at a theater to be announced.
Compiled with the assistance of Suzanne O'Connor.

Late Edition - Final

SECTION: Section AR; Column 0; Arts and Leisure Desk; Pg. 64

LENGTH: 2034 words

HEADLINE: 'We Are the Establishment Now'

BYLINE: By MANOHLA DARGIS and A. O. SCOTT

 BODY:
When Joel and Ethan Coen were growing up, they used to make Super 8 films, including remakes of Hollywood movies that they had watched on TV. Since then, the Coens have continued to let their imagination loose while ranging wide across cinematic and literary genres, drawing on pulp literature (''Blood Simple,'' ''Miller's Crossing''), screwball cinema (''The Hudsucker Proxy,'' ''Intolerable Cruelty''), Homer's Odyssey (''O Brother, Where Art Thou?''), the Bible and their own suburban Minneapolis childhood (''A Serious Man'') to make movies that, increasingly, have the quality of an evolving, distinctly American mythopoeia. 
In July, Manohla Dargis and A. O. Scott, the chief film critics for The New York Times, met with Joel Coen, now 58, and Ethan Coen, 55, in Santa Monica, Calif., to discuss their work, including their latest film, ''Inside Llewyn Davis'' (Dec. 6) about a 1961 folk singer (played by Oscar Isaac), who's struggling with his music, the world, the changing times and -- this being the Coens -- his comically flawed humanity. The brothers sat across from each other and at times seemed to share a single (very large and fast-moving) brain. These are excerpts from the conversation.
Q. Can you talk about the genesis of ''Inside Llewyn Davis''?
Ethan Coen We were in the office and Joel said, ''O.K., suppose Dave Van Ronk gets beat up outside of Gerde's Folk City. That's the beginning of a movie.''
Joel Coen It was an idea we kept coming back to. We were thinking 1961 is interesting, because it's the scene that Dylan came into, not the one he created or transformed, because people know more about that. Dylan once said something -- and I'm paraphrasing him -- ''Really, all I wanted is to be as big as Dave Van Ronk.'' That's how limited that scene was, in terms of the people in the broader culture.
Did you grow up listening to that music?
Ethan Yeah, sort of, through Bob Dylan, like everybody else, probably.
Joel I'm a little older than Ethan, but I have very vague early memories of hearing folk music, my mother playing it or something, when I was fairly little.
Ethan We had a fantastic record of a concert, a rock concert, of Big Bill Broonzy and Pete Seeger, which is kind of ----
Joel Yes, that was a really early record that was a concert in Chicago, where Pete played. In fact, that's how we met T Bone [Burnett]. T Bone called us up sort of out of the blue after he saw ''Raising Arizona,'' because he thought it was very amusing that we played Beethoven on banjo in the score in the movie. We said to him, ''Well, we really stole that from Pete Seeger,'' because Pete Seeger played that. We knew it from this concert record.
Ethan Oh, the one disclaimer I should've made when we talked about the genesis of the movie: We did start thinking about Dave Van Ronk, and in fact read his memoir, which is kind of great, ''The Mayor of Macdougal Street.'' But the movie's not about Dave Van Ronk, although Oscar, the character, has his kind of repertoire. It's his music. It's a fictional character we gave his music to.
How did you find Oscar Isaac?
Joel After we wrote the movie and we started casting it, we knew that there was going to be a lot of performance in the movie, and that actually when you heard a song in the movie, we really wanted to hear the whole song. And it's also a story where we felt like there's got to be something about the character that you only know through his performance and his music -- you know, like a real musician. So we only auditioned real musicians.
Ethan [Laughs.]
Joel You know, that was not so great. It's often possible -- sometimes it's even easy -- to get somebody like that through a scene or two scenes or three scenes or whatever, and it's great, it's fine. But this character's literally in every scene in the movie, so we realized we were going the wrong direction, and we just started seeing actors who could play, as opposed to musicians who could act. And there are more of those, by the way.
Ethan And we've been doing this like, 30 years. You'd think we know something as basic as this, that you need an actor.
Joel I know. It was a little insane. Oscar came in and he said, ''Most actors, if you ask them if they play guitar, they'll say they played guitar for 20 years, but what they really mean is they've owned a guitar for 20 years.'' Oscar's actually played guitar since he was little little, you know? He played, and we sent the tape to T Bone, and T Bone said, ''This guy's actually a better musician than a lot of the studio guys I work with.'' So we went, ''We found him.''
Can we talk a little bit about how you write? Are you locked in a room together?
Joel It's always been the same. We don't split it up. You know: ''You write this scene. You write that.'' There's a lot of just sitting around talking ideas before we start writing anything. So there's kind of a long period of that, and then we generally start at the beginning and just kind of start hammering the scenes out. We don't outline stuff or any of that, although there are some movies where we kind of have a pretty good idea of the shape of it. Or we even know how it's going to end when we start, and then others where we just have no idea whatsoever, and it goes where it goes. They don't all ----
Ethan This one actually ----
Joel Don't get finished. They get put aside for a while, you know.
Ethan The rule is: It goes where it goes, although in the case of this one, we kind of knew either at the beginning or near the very beginning, that it was going to circle back.
Who's typing?
Ethan Usually me, because I type faster, but sometimes Joel. We'll just talk the scene back and forth, and occasionally, one of us will sit down and say, ''Oh, wait, I've got something.'' If it's an idea that consists of a few lines, an exchange, one of us will type without consulting the other.
Are you comfortable throwing out each other's bad ideas?
Ethan Oh, sure, yeah.
Joel Perfectly comfortable, very comfortable. But you know, someone asked us once how we adapt novels, and Ethan said, ''Joel holds the book open by the spine, while I retype it into the computer.''
Your recent adaptations [of ''True Grit'' by Charles Portis and ''No Country for Old Men'' by Cormac McCarthy] are remarkably faithful.
Ethan Yeah, they're really good books, man.
Joel Don't change it if it's not broken!
Has working together changed over the 30 years?
Joel Not really.
Have other things changed?
Joel Well, the craft of it's changed a lot, just because of digital technology. That's the thing that's been the most radical. I mean, outside of that, it's still the same as when we were making Super 8 movies, basically. This movie was not shot digitally. We shot it on film. It's probably ----
Ethan Probably the last one.
Joel It might be the last one we ever do on film.
Ethan ''True Grit'' was the last film that Roger Deakins shot on film.
Joel We were one of the last people to stop cutting on film. And when we stopped, people would say, ''Why?'' Honestly, the answer was because we couldn't find assistants who knew how to work on film. They didn't exist anymore. I mean, it was -- I remember being in Ken Loach's cutting room around then, and I said -- he was cutting on a Steenbeck back then -- and I said, ''How do you do this?'' And he pointed like that [points] and there was this, like, 96-year-old guy on the rewinds.
When did you make the shift to digital editing?
Ethan ''Intolerable Cruelty'' was the first one we cut.
Have you tried out any digital cameras yet?
Ethan We've seen Roger's tests of the Alexa, which are pretty remarkable, which is the eerie thing.
Joel I think both of us -- and T Bone I would throw in here, too -- are very sort of analog. I'd rather listen to vinyl than to a CD. I'd rather see a movie shot on film. I don't think they look the same. I think you can duplicate things with digital technology, but what you end up doing is trying to recapture elements of photochemical technology that aren't there, and they always look a little screwy.
Ethan The analog texture feels so good.
Joel There was a period of time when you could choose whether you were shooting in black and white or in color, and depending on the subject matter -- and usually it's sort of genre-driven and all the rest. It would be great if you could say, ''This movie lends itself to digital shooting, this one, black and white,'' without there being any kind of arty stigma put on it. It's just another thing you can try.
You make movies that are released by studios and win Oscars, yet on some level, you are still not mainstream guys. It's kind of a contradictory position.
Joel Yeah, I know. You know, we've talked about that ourselves. It's weird.
Ethan Yeah. If you'll pardon me, the indie thing might all just be a journalists' thing. James Cameron makes huge movies that are what he wants to do, and they're financed and released by studios. Indie is like, a term of praise that you use for us, fortunately, and some of you bash Jim Cameron for not being, although he kind of is.
But certainly there were people who were making movies outside of the studio system.
Joel Maybe that's more what it is. Because when we started out, it was just the default place because no one would give us money to make a movie. We would've taken it if it had been offered when we were making our first movie. We would've gone, ''Is there somebody who will pay for this in a big Hollywood studio?'' We were moving toward the indie thing, but in another way we were always hoping a big studio would release the movies. And there was a point where we looked at each other, and we went, ''I guess we're kind of the mainstream guys.'' You know, when we won the Oscar.
Ethan That's true.
Joel We said, ''How'd that happen?''
Ethan We are the establishment now.
Some of that is generational.
Ethan I was just talking about that, actually, with our dear friend Steven Spielberg. The three of us ----
Joel It's true. It's true. Because even he's had to go: ''How did I get here? Why am I the establishment guy?'' -- you know?
Ethan Is there a way to half-put irony quotes around the ''dear friend'' thing?[Laughter.]
Since you mention him: He and George Lucas recently talked about the state of the movie business and how frustrated they are with it. Do you have thoughts on it?
Ethan God, I don't know. What do you say? I don't know. What do you say?
A lot. Or nothing.
Ethan You know, moaning about that stuff: it might be true, and it might be a hallmark of getting old, and it might be both. You know? Isn't that what you complain about when you get old, about how it's harder?
It's a recurrent theme -- the crisis in film.
Joel It's definitely harder now. On the other hand, I think you can exaggerate that, too, because the movie business in the United States, despite the sort of ups and downs of the economy, is still a very healthy business. And that healthy business is going to support -- and it always has -- a lot of niche moviemaking. More than you might expect it to, given the mentality. There's still a lot of interesting stuff being made which is completely outside of the kind of trend that we're describing, you know?
Ethan We've always actually been remarkably commercially successful. Not in terms of making huge amounts of money, which we rarely do, but in terms of not losing money and making modest amounts of money. We're actually strangely consistent in that respect. We've been able to keep making movies because of that and also because, strangely, we've had studio patrons, starting from Barry Diller. Sometimes they're establishment people who know they're not going to make huge amounts of money, but they like your movies. They're moviegoers, too.
Joel And mostly they're making blockbusters, but when you get in a room with them, they go, ''Go off and make your movie, and I'll do it as long as I can't get hurt too bad.'' You know? They're completely open to that still. They don't want to get burned.
Ethan They don't want to look stupid.
Joel Nobody wants to look stupid or lose lots of money. On the other hand, they're not afraid of doing other stuff if they can trust you to keep it reasonable. So, yeah, they kind of let us wander off without any adult supervision and do what we want.

Late Edition - Final

SECTION: Section BU; Column 0; Money and Business/Financial Desk; Pg. 5

LENGTH: 572 words

HEADLINE: Reader Comments

 BODY:
''YOU FOR SALE,'' a series of articles on the business of personal data, turned last Sunday to how one company was opening part of its vast data vault to consumers.
The article, ''A Data Broker Offers a Peek Behind the Curtain,'' described how the Acxiom Corporation was creating a free Web site, Aboutthedata.com, where people can view some information that the company has collected about them.
Unveiled on Wednesday, the site lets consumers view information like biographical facts, homeownership and vehicle details, categories of recent purchases and household interests. It also allows people to opt out entirely from having Acxiom collect and store marketing data about them. 
Readers had varying reactions to plans for the site, and to the idea of data collection in general. Comments have been edited and condensed from nytimes.com:
I think each of us should own our data and have fine-grained control on how it is used. Right now, companies like Acxiom profit by selling our data. Instead, consumers should receive more of the benefits. Most consumers would be happy to sell their data on a case-by-case basis in return for case-by-case benefits (coupons, discounts, etc.). But this ''sale'' should be single-use. I might be willing to sell my data with blanket permission to share and reuse it indefinitely, but the price tag would be quite high.
I would gladly share my ''data income'' with data brokers in return for assistance in providing me a single place to correct errors and services in auctioning my data in accordance with my preferences. Aboutthedata.com is a good first step.
FREQUENT FLYER, USA
Think about it this way: When I was a child, the clerk at the cash register was a fixture at the store -- knew our names or could associate us with our parents. And a really good clerk could actually help us in the store. Contrast that with what we have now and you might consider the coupons and mailers as an effort to replace that experience, albeit in a much less -- and, at times, seemingly much more -- personal way. What makes me angry is poor handling and sharing of that info with others.
MARGARET, ATLANTA
Reveal ''some'' of the info? What good is that?
I would want all of the information, not just what they want to let me know they have and sell about me.
ACHILLESMJB, NYC, NY
You'd better believe I am opting out on Day 1, period.
PALE FIRE, BOSTON
I find this a rather interesting proposition and will log in to check it out. This data collection is already happening, but now we can be a part of the process, which is a thought I find refreshing.
I am O.K. with giving out some of my information and would like to hear about targeted products and services that may match my interests based on my demographic, past purchase behavior, etc.
M, BROOKLYN
Have you ever filled out an information form for a store and gotten a store registration card, such as at your grocery store, gas station, etc.? You just waived the personal part. Where do you think that data goes? Yes, you get a few bargain prices, but the store collects your purchasing record forever. So, too, with credit cards, etc.
Want part of the profit? Buy Acxiom stock.JAY26, PA.
If it is my information that Axciom and others are selling, how come I am not getting any money from those transactions? It seems to me that the way the economy is being structured in the 21st century, we consumers need to profit from our personal information, too.
ROBB, WASHINGTON
